Ash'arite Occasionalist Explanation of Flotation and Sinking
States the Ash'arite occasionalist view on flotation and sinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite occasionalist account of flotation and sinking as direct divine actions, not natural causal processes.

Core Idea
Flotation and sinking are direct acts of God, not natural properties.

The Ash'arites reject natural causality and explain all physical phenomena as direct creations of God. In this passage, they apply this principle to the everyday phenomena of floating and sinking, asserting that God directly creates rest in floating bodies and motion in sinking bodies, all according to His habitual course.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'arite position
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms: sukun (rest), harakat (motions), and mubayanat (disparities).
The passage states the Ash'arite view concisely.
2
Identify the three divine acts
Find that floating is caused by a rest created by God, sinking by motions and disparities created by God.
The water's role is also created by God.
3
Relate to the principle of habitual course
Understand that these events occur according to God's habit (jarī al-‘āda), not natural necessity.
4
Reflect on why this was not mentioned in the book
Consider why the author says this is obvious from their principle.
This shows the consistency of Ash'arite thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراضان المذكوران عليه ثم اعلم أن الحق عند الأشاعرة هو أن الطفو إنما يكون بسبب سكون يخلقه الله تعالى في الجسم فيقتضي اختصاصه بحيزه، والرسوب إنما هو بسبب حركات يخلقها الله في الراسب ومباينات يخلقها الله في أجزاء الماء على طريقة جري العادة. وإنما لم يذكر في الكتاب لأنه معلوم من قاعدتهم المشهورة ( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ( للهواء اعتماد صاعد لازم ويلزمه أن لا يصعد ولا يطفو الخشبة ) على الماء ) بل ينفصل الهواء منها ويصعد ) ويطفو وحده على الماء ( كما ذكرنا ) إذ لا سبب لطفو الخشبة إلا تشبث الهواء بها وإذا كان الهواء متصعداً بالطبع أن ينفصل عما هو متسفل بالطبع فيطفو المتصعد ويرسب المتسفل ( وقد عرفت ما فيه وهو أنه ربما كان التركيب أو الوضع موجباً للتلازم ومانعاً عن الانفصال ( كيف ) أي كيف لا يتوجه عليه ما قد عرفته ؟ ( والهواء الذي فيه ) أي في الخشب لم يبق على كيفيته المقتضية للانفصال والصعود بل انكسر كيفيته بالامتزاج أو الاختلاط التام فلا ينفصل حينئذ حتى يرسب الخشب في الماء، ( ومنعه ابنه وقال ليس للهواء اعتماد لازم لا علوي ولا سفلي بل اعتماده مجتلب) بسبب محرك ( ويرد عليه أنَّ الزق المنفوخ فيه ( المقسور تحت الماء إذا خلي) وطبعه يصعد بما يتعلق به من جسم ثقيل إذا كان بحيث يقوى ذلك الزق على تحريكه وتصعيده ( ولو حل وكاؤه شق) الهواء الذي فيه ( الماء وخرج) منه (فلولا اعتماده الصاعد لم يكن كذلك وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون ذلك ) الصعود والخروج لضغط الماء له وإخراجه من ذلك الموضع بثقل وطأته ) وقوة عصره إياه وهو مدفوع
كلامه بل بالنسبة إلى الماء ويقيد إيجابهما للرسوب والطفو بأن لم يمنع عنه مانع فبإرادة الثقل والخية بالنسبة إلى الماء اندفع الاعتراض الثاني لأنَّ ألف من حبة خشباً ليس أثقل من حديد وإن كان أكثر وزناً منه وبالتقييد بعدم المانع اندفع الاعتراض الأول وهو ظاهر.
قوله : (بما يتعلق به إلخ) هذا التقييد للمبالغة في صعوده والوكاء بكسر الواو ما يشد به رأس الزق والوطأة بوزن الفعلة الضغطة أو الأحذة الشديدة .
قوله : ( ويرد عليه أن الزق إلخ الظاهر أن هذا معارضة وإن لم يكن دليل المعلل الأول مذكوراً والوكاء بكسر الواو والمد الذي يشد به رأس القربة . قوله : ( لضغط الماء ضغطه يضغطه ضغطاً زحمه إلى حائط ونحوه ومنه ضغطة القبر وهذا النظر الذي أورده المصنف إشارة إلى ما ذهب إليه قوم من أنَّ العناصر كلها طالبة لمركز العالم لكن الأثقل يسبق الأخف فيضغطه ويدفعه إلى فوق ولذلك يطفو الأخف فوقه. وقد رده الشيخ في الإشارات صريحاً بما ذكره الشارح حيث قال : من ظن أنه الهواء يطفو فوق الماء لضغط
The two objections mentioned against him. Then know that the truth according to the Ash‘arites is that floating occurs only due to a rest (sukūn) that God creates in the body, which necessitates its occupation of its place, and sinking occurs only due to motions (ḥarakāt) that God creates in the sinking body and disparities (mubāyanāt) that God creates in the parts of water, in the manner of the course of habit (majrā al-‘āda). And it was not mentioned in the book because it is known from their famous principle. And among them is that he (al-Jubbā'ī) said: Air has a necessary upward tendency (i‘timād ṣā‘id lāzim), and it follows that it does not ascend nor does the wood float on water, but rather the air separates from it and ascends and floats alone on the water, as we mentioned, for there is no cause for the floating of wood except the attachment of air to it. And since air is naturally ascending, it must separate from that which is naturally descending, so the ascending floats and the descending sinks. And you have known what is in it: that perhaps the composition or arrangement necessitates co-existence and prevents separation. How? That is, how would it not be objected to by what you have known? And the air that is in it (i.e., the wood) has not remained with its quality necessitating separation and ascent, but its quality has been broken by mixing or complete intermingling, so it does not separate then, so that the wood sinks in water. And his son (Abū Hāshim) denied it and said: Air does not have a necessary tendency, neither upward nor downward, but its tendency is acquired (mujtalab) due to a mover. And it is objected against him that the inflated bag (al-ziq al-manfūkh fīh) when forced under water, if released, rises by nature, pulling with it a heavy body attached to it, if the bag is strong enough to move it and raise it. And if its tie is undone, the air in it splits the water and exits from it. Were it not for its ascending tendency, it would not be so. And in this there is consideration because it is possible that the rising and exiting are due to the pressure of the water on it, ejecting it from that place by the weight of its pressure and the force of its squeezing. And it is refuted... His statement: 'but relative to water' and it restricts their requiring sinking and floating by there being no preventing obstacle. So by intending weight and lightness relative to water, the second objection is repelled, because a thousand grains of wood are not heavier than iron, even if they have more weight than it. And by the restriction of the absence of an obstacle, the first objection is repelled, and it is obvious. His statement: 'attached to it' — this restriction is for exaggeration in its ascent. And 'al-wikā'' (with kasr of wāw) is what the head of the bag is tied with. And 'al-waṭ'a' (like the measure of fi‘la) means the pressing or the severe shock. His statement: 'and it is objected against him that the bag etc.' — apparently this is a counterargument even if the evidence of the first arguer is not mentioned. And 'al-wikā'' (with kasr of wāw and madd) is what the head of the waterskin is tied with. His statement: 'for the pressure of the water' — 'ḍaghṭ' means he pushes it against a wall etc., and among it is the pressing of the grave. And this consideration that the author presented is a reference to what a group held: that all elements seek the center of the world, but the heavier precedes the lighter, so it pushes it and drives it upward, and therefore the lighter floats above it. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) refuted it explicitly in his 'Ishārāt' by what the commentator mentioned, where he said: Whoever thinks that air floats above water due to pressure...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash‘arite position on flotation and sinking, which is thoroughly occasionalist: God directly creates rest in the floating body and motions in the sinking body, along with disparities in the water, all according to habitual course. The author notes that this was not mentioned in the book because it follows from their well-known principle. The passage then discusses al-Jubbā'ī's view that air has a necessary upward tendency, which would imply that wood cannot float because the air inside it would separate and ascend. The response is that the air in wood is not in its pure state; due to mixing, it does not separate. Al-Jubbā'ī's son Abū Hāshim denied that air has any necessary tendency and held it is acquired from a mover. An objection is raised using an inflated bag: when submerged and released, it rises and can even lift a heavy object; if untied, the air bursts out. This would disprove that air has no ascending tendency. However, a counterargument suggests that the rise may be due to water pressure rather than an inherent tendency. The author concludes by clarifying that the earlier discussion about weight and lightness is relative to water and is restricted by the absence of obstacles, which repels both objections. The commentator then explains technical terms and notes that this consideration of pressure corresponds to a view held by some that heavier elements push lighter ones upward.

الطفو
الرسوب
السكون
الحركة
طريقة جري العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Floating and sinking are caused by natural properties like weight and density.
Correction: The Ash'arite view holds that they are directly created by God, not by inherent natural properties.

Checkpoint

B) The upward pressure of water
C) A rest (sukūn) created by God in the body
D) The shape of the object

Key Takeaway
For the Ash'arites, flotation and sinking are direct divine acts, not natural consequences of physical properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see an object float or sink, do you truly perceive it as a direct act of God in this moment, or do you instinctively attribute it to natural properties? If you are honest, what does that reveal about the state of your tawhid? What would it take for you to genuinely see every physical event as God's constant creation?
?
If floating and sinking are not inherent but directly sustained by God, then your very existence—your breath, your heartbeat—is being created from moment to moment. How does this reality change the way you will greet the next moment of your life? Will you consciously acknowledge His direct involvement?
?
This passage teaches that even water's behavior is a habit of God, not a necessary law. Are you living as though God could break that habit at any moment, or have you become complacent, trusting in nature rather than the Creator? What is one area of your life where you most need to cultivate this awareness today?

Ash'arite Refutation of Al-Jubba'i's Theory of Air's Upward Tendency
Presents al-Jubba'i's theory and its refutation through multiple arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite critique of al-Jubba'i's theory of air's necessary upward tendency and the key arguments involving composition, Abu Hashim's denial, the inflated bag, and water pressure.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i's necessary upward tendency of air is refuted by showing that air in mixtures loses its character and that the bag argument can be explained by water pressure.

The passage now turns to a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazilite scholar al-Jubba'i, who argued that air possesses a necessary upward tendency. This leads into a multi-layered debate including objections from his son Abu Hashim, a physical example with an inflated bag, and a counterargument based on water pressure. The discussion illustrates the Ash'arite method of engaging with opposing views within a kalam framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Jubba'i's position
Read al-Jubba'i's claim that air has a necessary upward tendency, which would imply wood cannot float because the air inside would separate and ascend.
Al-Jubba'i was a Mu'tazilite scholar.
2
Study the Ash'arite response
Notice that the response argues that the air in wood is mixed, so its quality is broken and it does not separate.
This appeals to composition as preventing separation.
3
Examine Abu Hashim's denial
Abu Hashim (al-Jubba'i's son) denied that air has any necessary tendency, holding it is acquired from a mover.
4
Analyze the bag argument
The inflated bag (ziq) is used to show that air does have an ascending tendency, but a counterargument attributes the rise to water pressure.
The counterargument is based on the idea of heavier elements pushing lighter ones.
5
Note the incomplete refutation
The passage ends with a statement that the counterargument is refuted, but the text cuts off before the full refutation is given.
This may be due to a break in the manuscript.

الاعتراضان المذكوران عليه ثم اعلم أن الحق عند الأشاعرة هو أن الطفو إنما يكون بسبب سكون يخلقه الله تعالى في الجسم فيقتضي اختصاصه بحيزه، والرسوب إنما هو بسبب حركات يخلقها الله في الراسب ومباينات يخلقها الله في أجزاء الماء على طريقة جري العادة. وإنما لم يذكر في الكتاب لأنه معلوم من قاعدتهم المشهورة ( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ( للهواء اعتماد صاعد لازم ويلزمه أن لا يصعد ولا يطفو الخشبة ) على الماء ) بل ينفصل الهواء منها ويصعد ) ويطفو وحده على الماء ( كما ذكرنا ) إذ لا سبب لطفو الخشبة إلا تشبث الهواء بها وإذا كان الهواء متصعداً بالطبع أن ينفصل عما هو متسفل بالطبع فيطفو المتصعد ويرسب المتسفل ( وقد عرفت ما فيه وهو أنه ربما كان التركيب أو الوضع موجباً للتلازم ومانعاً عن الانفصال ( كيف ) أي كيف لا يتوجه عليه ما قد عرفته ؟ ( والهواء الذي فيه ) أي في الخشب لم يبق على كيفيته المقتضية للانفصال والصعود بل انكسر كيفيته بالامتزاج أو الاختلاط التام فلا ينفصل حينئذ حتى يرسب الخشب في الماء، ( ومنعه ابنه وقال ليس للهواء اعتماد لازم لا علوي ولا سفلي بل اعتماده مجتلب) بسبب محرك ( ويرد عليه أنَّ الزق المنفوخ فيه ( المقسور تحت الماء إذا خلي) وطبعه يصعد بما يتعلق به من جسم ثقيل إذا كان بحيث يقوى ذلك الزق على تحريكه وتصعيده ( ولو حل وكاؤه شق) الهواء الذي فيه ( الماء وخرج) منه (فلولا اعتماده الصاعد لم يكن كذلك وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون ذلك ) الصعود والخروج لضغط الماء له وإخراجه من ذلك الموضع بثقل وطأته ) وقوة عصره إياه وهو مدفوع
كلامه بل بالنسبة إلى الماء ويقيد إيجابهما للرسوب والطفو بأن لم يمنع عنه مانع فبإرادة الثقل والخية بالنسبة إلى الماء اندفع الاعتراض الثاني لأنَّ ألف من حبة خشباً ليس أثقل من حديد وإن كان أكثر وزناً منه وبالتقييد بعدم المانع اندفع الاعتراض الأول وهو ظاهر.

The two objections mentioned against him. Then know that the truth according to the Ash‘arites is that floating occurs only due to a rest (sukūn) that God creates in the body, which necessitates its occupation of its place, and sinking occurs only due to motions (ḥarakāt) that God creates in the sinking body and disparities (mubāyanāt) that God creates in the parts of water, in the manner of the course of habit (majrā al-‘āda). And it was not mentioned in the book because it is known from their famous principle. And among them is that he (al-Jubbā'ī) said: Air has a necessary upward tendency (i‘timād ṣā‘id lāzim), and it follows that it does not ascend nor does the wood float on water, but rather the air separates from it and ascends and floats alone on the water, as we mentioned, for there is no cause for the floating of wood except the attachment of air to it. And since air is naturally ascending, it must separate from that which is naturally descending, so the ascending floats and the descending sinks. And you have known what is in it: that perhaps the composition or arrangement necessitates co-existence and prevents separation. How? That is, how would it not be objected to by what you have known? And the air that is in it (i.e., the wood) has not remained with its quality necessitating separation and ascent, but its quality has been broken by mixing or complete intermingling, so it does not separate then, so that the wood sinks in water. And his son (Abū Hāshim) denied it and said: Air does not have a necessary tendency, neither upward nor downward, but its tendency is acquired (mujtalab) due to a mover. And it is objected against him that the inflated bag (al-ziq al-manfūkh fīh) when forced under water, if released, rises by nature, pulling with it a heavy body attached to it, if the bag is strong enough to move it and raise it. And if its tie is undone, the air in it splits the water and exits from it. Were it not for its ascending tendency, it would not be so. And in this there is consideration because it is possible that the rising and exiting are due to the pressure of the water on it, ejecting it from that place by the weight of its pressure and the force of its squeezing. And it is refuted... His statement: 'but relative to water' and it restricts their requiring sinking and floating by there being no preventing obstacle. So by intending weight and lightness relative to water, the second objection is repelled, because a thousand grains of wood are not heavier than iron, even if they have more weight than it. And by the restriction of the absence of an obstacle, the first objection is repelled, and it is obvious. His statement: 'attached to it' — this restriction is for exaggeration in its ascent. And 'al-wikā'' (with kasr of wāw) is what the head of the bag is tied with. And 'al-waṭ'a' (like the measure of fi‘la) means the pressing or the severe shock. His statement: 'and it is objected against him that the bag etc.' — apparently this is a counterargument even if the evidence of the first arguer is not mentioned. And 'al-wikā'' (with kasr of wāw and madd) is what the head of the waterskin is tied with. His statement: 'for the pressure of the water' — 'ḍaghṭ' means he pushes it against a wall etc., and among it is the pressing of the grave. And this consideration that the author presented is a reference to what a group held: that all elements seek the center of the world, but the heavier precedes the lighter, so it pushes it and drives it upward, and therefore the lighter floats above it. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) refuted it explicitly in his 'Ishārāt' by what the commentator mentioned, where he said: Whoever thinks that air floats above water due to pressure...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash‘arite position on flotation and sinking, which is thoroughly occasionalist: God directly creates rest in the floating body and motions in the sinking body, along with disparities in the water, all according to habitual course. The author notes that this was not mentioned in the book because it follows from their well-known principle. The passage then discusses al-Jubbā'ī's view that air has a necessary upward tendency, which would imply that wood cannot float because the air inside it would separate and ascend. The response is that the air in wood is not in its pure state; due to mixing, it does not separate. Al-Jubbā'ī's son Abū Hāshim denied that air has any necessary tendency and held it is acquired from a mover. An objection is raised using an inflated bag: when submerged and released, it rises and can even lift a heavy object; if untied, the air bursts out. This would disprove that air has no ascending tendency. However, a counterargument suggests that the rise may be due to water pressure rather than an inherent tendency. The author concludes by clarifying that the earlier discussion about weight and lightness is relative to water and is restricted by the absence of obstacles, which repels both objections. The commentator then explains technical terms and notes that this consideration of pressure corresponds to a view held by some that heavier elements push lighter ones upward.

الجُبَّائي
الزق
ضغط الماء
التركيب
الانفصال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Air inherently tends upward, and this is proven by the inflated bag experiment.
Correction: The Ash'arite view holds that the rise of the bag could be due to water pressure, not an inherent tendency of air.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite response in the passage, why doesn't the air in a piece of wood separate and ascend?
A) The wood is too heavy
B) The air is mixed and its quality is broken
C) The air has no upward tendency
D) Water pressure prevents it

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite refutation of al-Jubba'i's theory of air's upward tendency relies on the idea that composition alters the nature of air, and that alternative explanations like water pressure can account for observed phenomena.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a long-held belief about yourself—like a character trait you thought was 'just the way you are'—could it be that the quality has been broken by mixing with influences you never examined? What would it take to restore the original purity of that quality, as the Ash'arites argued about air in wood?
?
The inflated bag argument seems convincing until an alternative explanation is offered. In your journey of faith, what is one evidence you've accepted without question, and what alternative explanation—possibly from the same sources—might you have overlooked? How willing are you to test your assumptions as rigorously as these scholars did?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Al-Jubba'i's Claim: Motion Generates Motion and Rest
Presents al-Jubba'i's claim and his empirical evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument that motion, not i'timad, is the sole generator of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion is the only cause; i'timad generates nothing.

Al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazili scholar, argues against the causal role of i'timad (reliance/inclination). He presents two everyday observations—the hand moving a key and a stone moving to rest—to claim that motion alone generates both motion and rest. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its reliance on empirical analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubba'i's statement
Read the Arabic passage where al-Jubba'i denies i'timad's generative power and asserts motion as the generator. Notice the examples he gives.
The examples are meant to be self-evident observations.
2
Identify the two observations
Al-Jubba'i uses two examples: the hand-key (motion generates motion) and the stone's rest (motion generates rest). Understand how each supports his claim.
The stone example covers both natural and forced motion.
3
Reflect on the commentator's clarification
The commentator notes that these examples are illustrations, not proofs. Ask yourself: why might al-Jubba'i consider them sufficient?
He may claim the principle is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ) لا يولد الاعتماد شيئاً لا حركة ولا سكوناً بل المولد لهما ) أي للحركة والسكون ( هو الحركة كما نشاهده أي نشاهد التوليد ( في حركة اليد لحركة (المفتاح فإنه ما لم تتحرك اليد لم يتحرك المفتاح فحركة المفتاح متولدة من حركة اليد لا من الاعتماد (و) كما نشاهده في حركة الحجر لسكونه في الموضع الذي يقصده الحجر (إما طبعاً أو قسراً ) فإنَّ ذلك السكون لا يتحصل ما لم يوجد حركته فهو متولد منها لا من الاعتماد الذي في الحجر، ( وقال ابنه : المولد لهما أي للحركة والسكون ) هو الاعتماد ) لا الحركة ( لوجهين الأول : أنه إذا أقيم عمود يمكن انتصابه قائماً على رأسه منفرداً فنصب كذلك ( وأدعم بدعامة ثم اعتمد عليه معتمد إلى جهة الدعامة لم يتحرك ) ذلك العمود إلى تلك الجهة ( فإن الدعامة تمنعه ذلك من ثم إذا أزيلت دعامته سقط إلى جهة الدعامة ) وإن لم يتحرك ذلك المعتمد إلى جهتها فعلمنا أن حركة العمود لم تتولد من الحركة بل من
قوله : (كما نشاهده إلخ تصوير للحكم الكلي بجزئي منه للإيضاح لا إثبات له به ولعله يدعي بداهته .
قوله : ( فعلمنا أن حركة العمود إلخ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ هذا الوجه ينفي مذهب الجبائي ولا يثبت مذهب أبي هاشم.
ثقل الماء إياه مجتمعاً تحته مثلاً لا بطبعه كذبه أنَّ الأكبر أقوى حركة وأسرع طفواً والقسري من خلاصة يكون بالضد هذا . قوله : ( للأصغر أقوى إذ لا شك أن دفعه إلى فوق الذي هو معنی الضغط أسهل وما قيل من أن الضغط إنما يكون عند شدة التكاثف بين الأجزاء وذلك بالكبر دون الصغر مما لا يلتفت إليه .
قوله: (لسكونه) اللام فيه وكذا في بالحركة المفتاح متعلقة بالضمير البارز في نشاهده باعتبار رجوعه إلى التوليد .
قوله: (لوجهين الأول إلخ) فيه تعرض لتوليد الاعتماد للحركة وليس فيه بيان توليد الاعتماد للسكون وقد يقال : إنما لم يتعرض له ادعاء لظهوره فإن سكون الحجر المرمي عندما ينزل إلى الأرض ليس بواسطة الحركة القسرية السابقة وهو ظاهر بل بواسطة الاعتماد على الأرض بقي فيه بحث وهو أن المدعى عام وهذا الدليل خاص إلا أن يحمل على أن المراد نفي مذهب الخصم. أعنى تولد جميع الحركات المتولدة من الحركة أو يدعى عدم الفرق بين هذه الصورة
And among them, he (al-Jubba'i) said: 'I'timad does not generate anything, neither motion nor rest; rather, what generates them (motion and rest) is motion, as we observe generation in the motion of the hand for the motion of the key, for as long as the hand does not move, the key does not move, so the motion of the key is generated from the motion of the hand, not from i'timad. And as we observe in the motion of the stone for its rest in the place that the stone aims at, either naturally or forcibly, for that rest is not obtained unless its motion occurs, so it is generated from it, not from the i'timad that is in the stone.' And his son said: 'What generates them (motion and rest) is i'timad, not motion, for two reasons: first, that if a column is erected that can stand upright on its head alone, and it is set up like that and supported by a support, then someone leaning leans on it towards the direction of the support, it does not move towards that direction, for the support prevents it from that; then when its support is removed, it falls towards the direction of the support, even if that leaning person did not move towards its direction. Thus we know that the motion of the column was not generated from motion, but from...' His saying: 'as we observe etc.' is a depiction of a universal judgment through a particular instance for clarification, not for proof; perhaps he claims its self-evidence. His saying: 'thus we know that the motion of the column etc.' contains an indication that this aspect negates al-Jubba'i's doctrine and does not establish Abu Hashim's doctrine. The weight of water on it, gathered under it, for example, not by its nature, he denied that the larger is stronger in motion and faster in floating, and the forcible, in summary, is the opposite. His saying: 'for the smaller is stronger' since there is no doubt that pushing it upward, which is the meaning of pressure, is easier, and what is said that pressure only occurs when there is intense condensation between parts, and that is with largeness not smallness, is something not paid attention to. His saying: 'for its rest'—the lam in it and similarly in 'by the motion of the key' is attached to the pronoun in 'we observe' considering its return to generation. His saying: 'for two reasons, first etc.' contains an exposition of i'timad's generation of motion, and there is no explanation of i'timad's generation of rest. And it may be said: he did not address it claiming its obviousness, for the rest of the thrown stone when it descends to the ground is not by way of the preceding forcible motion, which is obvious, but rather by way of i'timad on the ground. There remains a discussion: that the claim is general and this proof is specific, unless it is interpreted that the intended meaning is to negate the opponent's doctrine, i.e., the generation of all motions from motion, or he claims that there is no difference between this form...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between al-Jubba'i (the father) and his son Abu Hashim on the causal role of i'timad (reliance/inclination). Al-Jubba'i argues that i'timad does not generate anything; rather, motion generates both motion and rest. He gives two examples: the hand moving a key (motion generates motion) and a stone moving to its natural or forced resting place (motion generates rest). His son counters that i'timad generates both motion and rest, and he offers a proof using a column: a column supported by a prop and then leaned upon does not move; when the prop is removed, it falls toward the direction of the lean, even though the leaning person did not move. This shows that the column's motion came from the i'timad (the leaning load), not from motion. The commentator then discusses the implications: the son's proof only refutes the father's claim that motion generates all motion, but does not itself prove that i'timad generates rest (it only shows it generates motion). The father could argue that the stone's rest is due to i'timad on the ground, not motion. The commentator notes that the son's argument is limited to denying the father's position, not establishing his own fully. The passage also includes marginal notes clarifying grammatical points and explaining that the son's proof is specific while the claim is general, leaving room for further debate.

Key Terms
توليد (tawleed)
اعتماد (i'timad)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think i'timad is a necessary cause for motion or rest, but al-Jubba'i argues it is irrelevant.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i's examples show that motion alone suffices to generate motion and rest, without invoking i'timad.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jubba'i claim is the generator of motion and rest?

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i denies i'timad any causal power, asserting that motion alone generates motion and rest, as shown by everyday examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a goal, do you secretly credit your own inner strength (i'timad) more than the mere act of moving? How would your humility—and your trust in Allah—deepen if you truly believed your i'timad generates nothing, and that only the motion itself (which Allah creates) is the means?
?
Al-Jubba'i uses everyday observations to argue that motion alone causes results. But you know as a believer that Allah is the ultimate Creator of causes and effects. In what area of your life are you most tempted to treat a visible cause (like your own effort) as the sole sufficient cause, forgetting the Creator behind it all?
?
If your i'timad—your inner resolve—is truly powerless to generate any effect, then what is the point of having intention? How does this lesson push you to rely not on your own confidence but on the action itself, which is sustained by Allah's power? What specific action can you take today to shift your reliance from your inner state to the actual movement of obedience?

Abu Hashim's Counter: I'timad Generates Motion
Presents Abu Hashim's counter-claim and his column proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument using a column to prove that i'timad generates motion, and the scope of this proof.

Core Idea
I'timad, not motion, generates motion in the column experiment.

Abu Hashim, al-Jubba'i's son, challenges his father's doctrine with a clever thought experiment involving a supported column. He argues that when a column is leaned upon, it does not move until its support is removed—revealing that the motion comes from the i'timad (the leaning load), not from any prior motion. This lesson examines his proof and the commentator's observation that it only refutes the father's claim, without fully establishing i'timad's role in generating rest.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's argument
Read the passage describing the column experiment. Understand the setup: a column is erected with a support (di'ama), then someone leans on it toward the support. Initially, the column does not move; when the support is removed, it falls toward the direction of the lean.
The key point: no motion occurs before removal, yet the column moves after removal.
2
Identify the conclusion
Abu Hashim concludes that the column's motion is generated by the i'timad (the leaning load) because there was no prior motion.
This directly contradicts al-Jubba'i's claim that motion generates motion.
3
Note the commentator's limitation
The commentator points out that this argument only negates al-Jubba'i's doctrine (that motion generates all motion) but does not prove that i'timad generates rest. It only shows i'timad generates motion in this scenario.
The proof is specific to motion generation, not rest.

قوله : (كما نشاهده إلخ تصوير للحكم الكلي بجزئي منه للإيضاح لا إثبات له به ولعله يدعي بداهته .
قوله : ( فعلمنا أن حركة العمود إلخ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ هذا الوجه ينفي مذهب الجبائي ولا يثبت مذهب أبي هاشم.
ثقل الماء إياه مجتمعاً تحته مثلاً لا بطبعه كذبه أنَّ الأكبر أقوى حركة وأسرع طفواً والقسري من خلاصة يكون بالضد هذا . قوله : ( للأصغر أقوى إذ لا شك أن دفعه إلى فوق الذي هو معنی الضغط أسهل وما قيل من أن الضغط إنما يكون عند شدة التكاثف بين الأجزاء وذلك بالكبر دون الصغر مما لا يلتفت إليه .
قوله: (لسكونه) اللام فيه وكذا في بالحركة المفتاح متعلقة بالضمير البارز في نشاهده باعتبار رجوعه إلى التوليد .
قوله: (لوجهين الأول إلخ) فيه تعرض لتوليد الاعتماد للحركة وليس فيه بيان توليد الاعتماد للسكون وقد يقال : إنما لم يتعرض له ادعاء لظهوره فإن سكون الحجر المرمي عندما ينزل إلى الأرض ليس بواسطة الحركة القسرية السابقة وهو ظاهر بل بواسطة الاعتماد على الأرض بقي فيه بحث وهو أن المدعى عام وهذا الدليل خاص إلا أن يحمل على أن المراد نفي مذهب الخصم. أعنى تولد جميع الحركات المتولدة من الحركة أو يدعى عدم الفرق بين هذه الصورة
And among them, he (al-Jubba'i) said: 'I'timad does not generate anything, neither motion nor rest; rather, what generates them (motion and rest) is motion, as we observe generation in the motion of the hand for the motion of the key, for as long as the hand does not move, the key does not move, so the motion of the key is generated from the motion of the hand, not from i'timad. And as we observe in the motion of the stone for its rest in the place that the stone aims at, either naturally or forcibly, for that rest is not obtained unless its motion occurs, so it is generated from it, not from the i'timad that is in the stone.' And his son said: 'What generates them (motion and rest) is i'timad, not motion, for two reasons: first, that if a column is erected that can stand upright on its head alone, and it is set up like that and supported by a support, then someone leaning leans on it towards the direction of the support, it does not move towards that direction, for the support prevents it from that; then when its support is removed, it falls towards the direction of the support, even if that leaning person did not move towards its direction. Thus we know that the motion of the column was not generated from motion, but from...' His saying: 'as we observe etc.' is a depiction of a universal judgment through a particular instance for clarification, not for proof; perhaps he claims its self-evidence. His saying: 'thus we know that the motion of the column etc.' contains an indication that this aspect negates al-Jubba'i's doctrine and does not establish Abu Hashim's doctrine. The weight of water on it, gathered under it, for example, not by its nature, he denied that the larger is stronger in motion and faster in floating, and the forcible, in summary, is the opposite. His saying: 'for the smaller is stronger' since there is no doubt that pushing it upward, which is the meaning of pressure, is easier, and what is said that pressure only occurs when there is intense condensation between parts, and that is with largeness not smallness, is something not paid attention to. His saying: 'for its rest'—the lam in it and similarly in 'by the motion of the key' is attached to the pronoun in 'we observe' considering its return to generation. His saying: 'for two reasons, first etc.' contains an exposition of i'timad's generation of motion, and there is no explanation of i'timad's generation of rest. And it may be said: he did not address it claiming its obviousness, for the rest of the thrown stone when it descends to the ground is not by way of the preceding forcible motion, which is obvious, but rather by way of i'timad on the ground. There remains a discussion: that the claim is general and this proof is specific, unless it is interpreted that the intended meaning is to negate the opponent's doctrine, i.e., the generation of all motions from motion, or he claims that there is no difference between this form...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between al-Jubba'i (the father) and his son Abu Hashim on the causal role of i'timad (reliance/inclination). Al-Jubba'i argues that i'timad does not generate anything; rather, motion generates both motion and rest. He gives two examples: the hand moving a key (motion generates motion) and a stone moving to its natural or forced resting place (motion generates rest). His son counters that i'timad generates both motion and rest, and he offers a proof using a column: a column supported by a prop and then leaned upon does not move; when the prop is removed, it falls toward the direction of the lean, even though the leaning person did not move. This shows that the column's motion came from the i'timad (the leaning load), not from motion. The commentator then discusses the implications: the son's proof only refutes the father's claim that motion generates all motion, but does not itself prove that i'timad generates rest (it only shows it generates motion). The father could argue that the stone's rest is due to i'timad on the ground, not motion. The commentator notes that the son's argument is limited to denying the father's position, not establishing his own fully. The passage also includes marginal notes clarifying grammatical points and explaining that the son's proof is specific while the claim is general, leaving room for further debate.

Key Terms
عمود ('amud)
دعامة (di'ama)
توليد (tawleed)
اعتماد (i'timad)
حركة (haraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the column experiment proves i'timad generates both motion and rest.
Correction: The experiment only proves that i'timad generates motion in this case; it does not address rest generation.

Checkpoint
In Abu Hashim's column experiment, what causes the column to fall when the support is removed?
A) The motion of the leaning person
B) The i'timad (leaning load)
C) The removal of support itself
D) The column's natural tendency

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim uses the column experiment to show that i'timad, not motion, can generate motion, directly refuting al-Jubba'i's claim, though the proof does not cover rest generation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often attribute visible actions (your 'motions') as the sole cause of outcomes—like your effort producing a result. This lesson shows that a hidden, static reliance (i'timad) can be the true generator. Where in your life have you mistakenly credited your hustle while ignoring your unspoken reliance on Allah? Will you pause today to specifically thank Him for the hidden support that holds you up?
?
The column did not move while the support was present, but the i'timad (lean) was still there. When the support vanished, the motion came from that constant leaning. Think of a time when a 'support' in your life was removed—a job, a relationship, a health. Did you see the resulting change as caused by the loss, or did you recognize that your underlying reliance on Allah (your spiritual i'timad) was always there and actually generated your movement forward? How can you strengthen that reliance before the supports are removed?
?
Abu Hashim's argument challenges the assumption that new motion must come from prior motion. Consider this: Do you live as though every good deed, every moment of guidance, originates from your own preceding action, or do you acknowledge that Allah places i'timad in your heart—a still, leaning trust—that then generates righteous actions? What would change if you saw your good deeds not as the cause but as the generated effect of your humble reliance on Him?

Commentator's Analysis: Scope and Limits of the Proof
Commentator's critical evaluation of Abu Hashim's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique that Abu Hashim's proof only refutes al-Jubba'i's doctrine without establishing i'timad's generation of rest, and the implications for the debate.

Core Idea
The proof negates the father's view but does not fully prove the son's claim.

After presenting Abu Hashim's argument, the commentator adds a crucial nuance: the column experiment only disproves al-Jubba'i's position that motion generates all motion; it does not prove that i'timad generates rest. This lesson examines the commentator's analysis, including the discussion of rest in the stone example and the general-versus-specific problem. Understanding this critique is essential for evaluating the strength of each side's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's remarks
Read the passage where the commentator discusses the limitation: 'this aspect negates al-Jubba'i's doctrine and does not establish Abu Hashim's doctrine.' Notice the distinction between negation and proof.
The commentator is highlighting the logical structure of the argument.
2
Understand the issue with rest generation
The commentator notes that Abu Hashim's proof only addresses motion generation, not rest. Then he discusses the stone example: al-Jubba'i could argue that the stone's rest is due to i'timad on the ground.
This shows that the debate on rest generation remains open.
3
Consider the general vs. specific problem
The commentator points out that Abu Hashim's claim is general (i'timad generates both motion and rest), but his proof is specific (only covers motion in one scenario). This is a logical weakness.
Unless Abu Hashim claims no difference between forms of motion/rest.

Al-Jubba'i: I'timad Does Not Generate; Motion Generates
Al-Jubba'i denies i'timad's generative role; motion alone generates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's position that i'timad is not a generative cause, and that motion alone generates both motion and rest, as illustrated by empirical examples.

Core Idea
I'timad does not generate anything; motion alone generates motion and rest.

Al-Jubba'i presents a distinctive view on causality: he denies that i'timad (reliance/inclination) has any generative power. Instead, he argues that motion itself is the sole generator of both motion and rest. In this passage, he provides two concrete examples to illustrate his claim, drawing on everyday observations to clarify his position. As you read, notice how he uses these examples to support his general rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the two examples: hand-key and stone-rest.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the two examples in the passage: (1) movement of the hand generating movement of a key, (2) movement of a stone generating its rest. Notice how each example illustrates the principle that motion generates, not i'timad.
The commentator notes these are illustrations, not proofs.
3
Understand the commentator's note
The commentator explains that al-Jubba'i uses particular instances to depict a universal judgment for clarification, not as proof. He may claim the principle is self-evident. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه قال (الجبائي): لا يولد الاعتماد شيئاً لا حركة ولا سكوناً بل المولد لهما أي للحركة والسكون هو الحركة كما نشاهده أي نشاهد التوليد في حركة اليد لحركة المفتاح فإنه ما لم تتحرك اليد لم يتحرك المفتاح فحركة المفتاح متولدة من حركة اليد لا من الاعتماد، وكما نشاهده في حركة الحجر لسكونه في الموضع الذي يقصده الحجر إما طبعاً أو قسراً فإن ذلك السكون لا يتحصل ما لم يوجد حركته فهو متولد منها لا من الاعتماد الذي في الحجر. قوله (كما نشاهده إلخ): تصوير للحكم الكلي بجزئي منه للإيضاح لا إثبات له به ولعله يدعي بداهته.
And among them is that he (al-Jubba'i) said: i'timad does not generate anything, neither motion nor rest; rather the generator of both (motion and rest) is motion, as we observe—that is, we observe generation—in the movement of the hand for the movement of the key, for as long as the hand does not move, the key does not move, so the key's movement is generated from the hand's movement and not from i'timad; and as we observe in the movement of the stone for its rest at the place it intends (by nature or by force), for that rest is not achieved unless its movement exists, so it is generated from it, not from the i'timad in the stone. His saying: (as we observe etc.): a depiction of the universal judgment by a particular instance of it for clarification, not to prove it; perhaps he claims its self-evidence.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's position on the generation (tawallud) of motion and rest. Al-Jubba'i argues that i'timad (reliance/inclination) generates nothing; rather, it is motion that generates both motion and rest. He gives two empirical examples: (1) The hand moving a key: the key's movement follows the hand's movement, so movement is generated by prior movement, not by any i'timad. (2) A stone moving to a place and then becoming still: the rest occurs only after the stone's movement, so rest is generated by that prior movement, not by i'timad. The commentator then notes that al-Jubba'i uses these examples as illustrations of the general principle, not as proofs (since he may claim the principle is self-evident). The passage thus establishes al-Jubba'i's view that i'timad is not a generative cause.

Key Terms
الاعتماد (al-iʿtimād): reliance, inclination
التوليد (al-tawallud): generation
الحركة (al-ḥarakah): motion
السكون (al-sukūn): rest
المولد (al-muwallid): generator

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Jubba'i denies any causal role for i'timad whatsoever.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i only denies that i'timad generates motion or rest; he does not deny that i'timad may have other effects.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, what generates motion and rest?
A) I'timad alone
B) Motion alone
C) Both i'timad and motion
D) Neither i'timad nor motion

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i argues that i'timad does not generate motion or rest; instead, motion itself is the sole generator of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do something, do you rely on your intention (i'timad) to bring about the action, or do you recognize that it is the actual motion that accomplishes it? How does this lesson challenge your understanding of cause and effect in your daily actions?
?
Al-Jubba'i argues that motion alone generates motion and rest, not i'timad. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes think that your good intentions alone suffice, without the motion of actual deeds? What does this passage teach you about the necessity of action over mere inclination?
?
If motion is the generator of both motion and rest, what does that imply about your own responsibility to move? Are you waiting for an inclination (i'timad) to act, or will you start moving and trust that the rest will follow?

Abu Hashim's First Proof: The Column and Support
First proof: column and support example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hashim's first proof demonstrates that i'timad generates motion and rest, using the example of a supported column.

Core Idea
Motion can arise from pressure (i'timad) without prior motion.

Abu Hashim argues that the cause of motion and rest is i'timad, not motion itself. His first proof uses a concrete physical example: a column that can stand on its own, a support, and a leaning object. By observing what happens when the support is removed, he shows that the column's fall is caused by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any prior motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof and its translation. Notice the setup: a column, a support, and a leaning object.
The passage uses the terms عمود (column) and دعامة (support).
2
Identify the logic
Follow the sequence: the column does not move when the support is present, even with the leaning object pushing. When the support is removed, the column falls toward the support.
The key is that the leaning object does not move further, so motion is not the cause.
3
Understand the conclusion
The column's motion (falling) is generated by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any motion. This supports the claim that i'timad generates motion and rest.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه: المولد لهما أي للحركة والسكون هو الاعتماد لا الحركة لوجهين: الأول: أنه إذا أقيم عمود يمكن انتصابه قائماً على رأسه منفرداً فنصب كذلك وأدعم بدعامة ثم اعتمد عليه معتمد إلى جهة الدعامة لم يتحرك ذلك العمود إلى تلك الجهة فإن الدعامة تمنعه ذلك، ثم إذا أزيلت دعامته سقط إلى جهة الدعامة وإن لم يتحرك ذلك المعتمد إلى جهتها فعلمنا أن حركة العمود لم تتولد من الحركة بل من الاعتماد. الثاني: حركة اليد متأخرة عن حركة الحجر إذ ما لم يتحرك مكانه امتنع حركة اليد إليه لامتناع التداخل بين الأجسام، والمتأخر لا يولد المتقدم وفيه نظر إذ لا تأخر هناك بحسب الزمان بل هما معاً بحسبه فلا يلزم التداخل، وأما بحسب الذات فحركة اليد متقدمة إذ يصح أن يقال: تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر ولا عكسه فجاز أن تكون حركة اليد مولدة لحركة الحجر.
And his son (Abu Hashim) said: The generator of both (motion and rest) is i'timad, not motion, for two reasons: First: if one erects a column that can stand upright on its own, and sets it up thus, and supports it with a support, and then a leaning object leans on it toward the direction of the support, that column does not move in that direction because the support prevents it. Then when its support is removed, it falls toward the direction of the support, even if the leaning object has not moved toward that direction. So we know that the column's movement was not generated from motion but from i'timad. Second: the hand's movement is posterior to the stone's movement; for as long as the stone's place has not moved, the hand's movement to it is impossible due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The posterior does not generate the prior. But there is a consideration: there is no temporal posteriority; both are simultaneous in time, so interpenetration does not follow. However, in terms of essence, the hand's movement is prior, for it is correct to say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved,' and not the reverse. So it is possible that the hand's movement generates the stone's movement.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's (son of al-Jubba'i) view that i'timad generates both motion and rest. He gives two proofs. First proof: A column is set upright and supported; a leaning object leans toward the support, but the column does not move because the support prevents it. When the support is removed, the column falls toward the support even without the leaning object moving further. This shows that the column's motion (falling) is generated by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any prior motion. Second proof: When a hand moves a stone, the hand's movement is temporally simultaneous with the stone's movement, so the hand's movement cannot generate the stone's due to the impossibility of interpenetration if one preceded. However, the commentator notes that temporal posteriority does not actually exist; they are simultaneous. In terms of essential priority, the hand's movement is prior (we say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved'), so it could generate movement. Thus Abu Hashim's second proof is criticised. The passage thus presents Abu Hashim's two arguments and a refutation of the second.

Key Terms
العمود (al-ʿamūd): column
الدعامة (al-diʿāmah): support
الاعتماد (al-iʿtimād): leaning/pressure

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Abu Hashim's first proof, what happens to the column when the support is removed?
A) It remains stationary
B) It falls toward the support
C) It falls away from the support
D) It moves in the direction of the leaning object

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's first proof shows that i'timad, not motion, generates motion in the column.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you felt 'supported' by something or someone, and when that support was removed, you 'fell' in a certain direction. What was the 'i'timad'—the hidden pressure or leaning—that determined that direction? Was it your attachment to that support, or something else? How does this lesson reveal that your movements may stem from unseen pressures, not just visible motions?
?
Abu Hashim's proof isolates i'timad as the cause of motion, even when the leaning object doesn't move. What 'leaning' in your heart is determining your actions, even when you feel still? Are you aware of the unseen pressures—like desires, fears, or habits—that are moving you? This lesson asks: what is the real i'timad that controls your direction?
?
The column fell toward the direction of the leaning object. In your spiritual life, what is your deepest leaning—toward Allah or toward created things? When the support of worldly pleasures or human approval is removed, which way do you fall? This proof challenges you to identify the true source of your spiritual motion.

Abu Hashim's Second Proof: Hand and Stone, and Its Critique
Second proof and its criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's second proof that i'timad generates motion, and why it is criticized on temporal and essential grounds.

Core Idea
Temporal simultaneity undermines the second proof, but essential priority might save it.

Abu Hashim's second proof argues that the hand's movement cannot generate the stone's movement because the hand moves after the stone, making it a posterior cause—which is impossible. However, the commentator points out that the hand and stone move simultaneously in time, so the fallacy of 'posterior generating prior' does not apply. Yet in terms of essential order, the hand's movement is prior, leaving the possibility that motion can generate motion after all.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the claim: the hand's movement is posterior to the stone's movement.
The term متأخر (posterior) and متقدم (prior) are key.
2
Understand the objection
Abu Hashim argues that if the hand moves after the stone, the hand cannot generate the stone's motion (the posterior cannot generate the prior).
3
Follow the critique
The commentator rejects the temporal posteriority: the hand and stone move simultaneously. But in essence, the hand is prior (we say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved'). This reopens the possibility that motion generates motion.
The critique does not fully destroy the second proof but weakens it.

Ibn 'Ayyash's Mediation on Generation
Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad; commentator rejects the theory of generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn 'Ayyash's mediating position on the generation of motion and rest, and how the commentator subjects the entire generative theory to critique.

Core Idea
Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad, but the theory itself is ultimately false.

Ibn 'Ayyash, a Basran theologian, attempts to reconcile the views of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim on the generation of motion and rest. He argues that since neither side's evidence proves exclusivity, we should allow generation from both motion and i'timad. However, the commentator reminds us that the entire theory of generation (tawallud) is false—a crucial metacritique that frames the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the key terms: mutamassak (evidence), inḥiṣār (restriction), tawallud (generation).
The passage is slightly fragmented due to OCR; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify Ibn 'Ayyash's position
Identify why Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad. He points out that each school's evidence indicates generation from its source without proving exclusivity.
The evidence of al-Jubba'i shows generation from motion; the evidence of his son (Abu Hashim) shows generation from i'timad.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator states that the whole theory of generation is false, so Ibn 'Ayyash's argument, built on that theory, is also false. Then al-Amidi concedes the theory for the sake of argument and critiques al-Jubba'i's specific claim.
This shows a layered approach: first a synthetic view, then a meta-critique.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the commentator would reject the entire generative framework. How does this impact the value of Ibn 'Ayyash's mediation?
This lesson connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology about causality and the rejection of secondary causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن عياش من البصريين بتولدهما أي بتولد الحركة والسكون من الحركة تارة ومن الاعتماد أخرى لمتمسكيهما، فإن متمسك الجبائي دل على تولدهما من الحركة من غير دلالة على انحصار تولدهما فيها، ومتمسك ابنه دل على تولد الحركة والسكون من الاعتماد بلا دلالة على الانحصار، فالصواب حينئذ تجويز تولدهما من كل واحد من الاعتماد والحركة. ولما كان القول بالتوليد باطلاً كما ستعرفه كان هذا الكلام المبني عليه باطلاً أيضاً، لكن الآمدي تنزل إلى صحة التوليد ثم ناقضهم فقال على الجبائي: كما أن حركة المفتاح متعقبة لحركة اليد كذلك هي متعقبة لاعتماد...
And Ibn 'Ayyash from the Basrans held that both (motion and rest) are generated—i.e., motion and rest are generated—sometimes from motion and sometimes from i'timad, due to the evidence for each side. For the evidence of al-Jubba'i indicates their generation from motion without indicating that their generation is restricted to it, and the evidence of his son indicates the generation of motion and rest from i'timad without indicating restriction. Therefore the correct view is to allow their generation from each of i'timad and motion. And since the doctrine of generation is false, as you will know, this statement built upon it is also false. However, al-Amidi conceded the validity of generation and then contradicted them, saying against al-Jubba'i: just as the movement of the key follows the hand's movement, so it follows i'timad...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage introduces Ibn 'Ayyash's mediating position: he accepts that motion and rest can be generated from either motion or i'timad, because each school (al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim) has evidence that generation occurs from one source but not exclusive evidence. So the correct view is to allow both possibilities. The commentator then steps back and notes that the entire theory of generation (tawallud) is ultimately false (as will be explained later). Nevertheless, al-Amidi (the commentator) provisionally accepts generation and then critiques al-Jubba'i's specific argument: the key's movement follows the hand's movement, but it also follows i'timad (since the hand has i'timad). The passage thus presents a synthetic view and a meta-critique of the generative framework.

Key Terms
ابن عياش (Ibn ʿAyyāsh): a Basran theologian
متمسك (mutamassak): evidence, proof
الانحصار (al-inḥiṣār): restriction, exclusivity
التوليد (al-tawallud): generation
الآمدي (al-Āmidī): the commentator

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn 'Ayyash's view is the final correct position in the debate.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the theory of generation is false, so Ibn 'Ayyash's argument is also false. The passage uses Ibn 'Ayyash's view to illustrate a flawed framework.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn 'Ayyash's position on whether motion and rest are generated?
A) Only motion can generate
B) Only i'timad can generate
C) Both motion and i'timad can generate
D) Neither can generate

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Ayyash attempts to reconcile the two Basran views on generation by allowing both sources, but the commentator ultimately rejects the entire theory of generation as false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn 'Ayyash tried to reconcile two flawed evidences by allowing both sources of generation, but the commentator rejected the entire theory as false. In your own life, when have you tried to reconcile ideas or habits that were built on a false foundation? What would it mean to let go of the entire framework rather than just adjusting it?
?
The passage shows that even respected theologians like al-Jubba'i had arguments that were incomplete (they did not prove exclusivity). Are you willing to admit that some of your deeply held beliefs might be based on incomplete evidence? Can you take one belief this week and examine whether your evidence for it is truly exclusive?
?
Al-Amidi provisionally accepted the theory of generation in order to critique it. The Generation of Downward Motion: Al-Jubba'i vs. His Son
Debate on generation of motion in a thrown stone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate on the generation of motion applies to a concrete example (a stone thrown upward) and why the author considers both positions lacking a decisive proof.

Core Idea
In kalām, even a falling stone reveals deep disagreements on causality.

This passage presents a specific case study from the debate on the generation (tawlid) of motion: a stone thrown upward by compulsion, which then falls back down. Al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim disagree on whether its downward motion is generated from the preceding upward motion or from a descending reliance. The author then critiques both views as arbitrary. Reading this will clarify how abstract principles play out in concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Al-Jubba'i's view, his son's view, then the author's comment.
The passage is cut off; we only have the beginning of the author's explanation.
2
Identify the two opposing principles
Al-Jubba'i says motion generates only from motion; his son says motion generates only from reliance (i'timad). See how each applies this to the stone.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author calls both views 'arbitrary judgment and weighting without preponderance'. Ask: why does he say that? What is missing?
This reveals the kalām standard of demanding a preponderant factor.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why this debate matters: it shows that even everyday phenomena can be interpreted differently based on metaphysical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ( في الحجر المرمي ) بالقسر ( إلى فوق إذا عاد هاوياً) أي نازلاً: أن حركته الهابطة متولدة من حركته الصاعدة ) بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الحركة لا من الاعتماد ) وقال ابنه : بل هي متولدة ( من الاعتماد الهابط ) ) الذي في الحجر بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الاعتماد لا من الحركة فلذلك قال المصنف : ( وهذا) فرع الخلاف الذي قبله ثم قال : ( وعلى الرأيين فيه تحكم وترجيح بلا مرجح ( أما الأول فلأنه إذا قيل : كل (حركة) من الحركات المتعاقبة
(And among them is that he said: (Al-Jubba'i (concerning the stone thrown) by compulsion (upward, when it returns descending) meaning falling: that its descending motion is generated from its ascending motion) based on his principle that motion is generated only from motion, not from reliance. And his son said: Rather, it is generated (from the descending reliance)) which is in the stone, based on his principle that motion is generated only from reliance, not from motion. Therefore, the author said: (And this) is a branch of the disagreement before it. Then he said: (And upon both views there is arbitrary judgment and weighting without preponderance. (As for the first, because if it is said: every (motion) of successive motions...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case in the debate on generation (tawlid) of motion. It cites the example of a stone thrown upward by compulsion (qasr) that then falls back down. Al-Jubba'i argues that the stone's downward motion is generated from its upward motion, adhering to his principle that motion can only be generated from motion, not from reliance (i'timad). His son Abu Hashim counters that the downward motion is generated from the downward reliance (i'timad hadit) inherent in the stone, following his own principle that motion is generated solely from reliance. The author (al-Iji) then comments that this case is merely a branch of the earlier disagreement on the general principle. He declares that both positions involve arbitrary judgment (tahakkum) and weighting without a preponderant factor (tarjih bila murajjih), indicating that neither side has a decisive proof for their exclusive claim. The passage ends with the beginning of an explanation of the first point ('As for the first, because if it is said: every motion of successive motions...').

Key Terms
اعتماد (i'timad)
توليد (tawlid)
حركة (haraka)
قسر (qasr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage describes a physical experiment or observable causality.
Correction: It is a metaphysical kalām argument about the generation of accidents (motion) and principles of causality, not empirical physics.

Checkpoint

A) His principle that motion is generated only from motion
B) His principle that motion is generated only from reliance
C) Direct observation of the stone's fall
D) The consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The author presents both positions on the stone's fall as arbitrary, highlighting the need for a preponderant factor in kalām arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i and his son each held strong positions based on their principles, yet the author says both are arbitrary. Are there core beliefs in your faith that you hold simply because you inherited them or because of loyalty to a scholar, without examining whether they have a clear proof? What would it take for you to honestly reassess one such belief this week?
?
The phrase 'weighting without a preponderant factor' (tarjih bila murajjih) exposes how we sometimes favor one view over another without solid grounds. Can you identify a situation in your daily life—perhaps in how you practice a religious obligation or interact with others—where you have strongly preferred a course of action without a compelling reason? How will you seek the missing preponderant factor?
?
If both sides in a theological debate can be arbitrary, how do you ensure that your own understanding of causality and God's actions is not based on mere assumption? What concrete step will you take today to root your creed more firmly in evidence from revelation and sound reason?

The Debate on Motion Generation: Motion vs. Reliance
Al-Jurjani presents the debate between al-Jubba'i and his son on whether motion or reliance generates motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement between al-Jubba'i and his son on the principle of generation (tawlīd) and how each uses examples to argue whether motion or reliance is the true cause of generated motion.

Core Idea
The debate over generation hinges on whether motion alone suffices or requires the principle of reliance.

This lesson presents a classic debate in the Basran Mu'tazila tradition between al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim. The question is: when one object moves another, is the generated motion caused by the motion itself or by an underlying inclination (i‘timād)? Through examples of a hand moving a key and a stone thrown upward, both sides present their principles. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the role of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the two opposing principles stated explicitly by al-Jubba'i and his son.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the argument.
2
Identify the two principles
Find the statements where al-Jubba'i says motion is independent in generation, and where his son says reliance is the source. Note how each principle is applied to the examples.
Look for phrases like 'بناء على أصله' (based on his principle).
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the hand-key example and the stone example. How does each side use these examples to support their principle? Then note the author's criticism that both views involve arbitrary assignment.
The author (al-Iji) calls both views 'taḥakkum' and 'tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ'.

Source Text
