Objection and Answer: Transparency and the Veil
Sanusi refines the argument by addressing transparent bodies and clarifying the veil's function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that transparent bodies do not occupy the sense, and the author's answer that colorlessness explains why celestial spheres do not veil.

Core Idea
Absolute transparency with no color does not veil; thin plates have color and thus veil.

After presenting the core argument, the author anticipates an objection: what about transparent bodies like the celestial spheres? They are thick but do not block vision. The author answers by distinguishing absolute transparency (no color) from plates that have some color. This lesson also clarifies that the thin plate aids weak eyes by refining the visual spirit, not because it is less of a veil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the phrase 'واعترض عليه' and understand the objection: transparent bodies like the spheres do not occupy the sense.
The objection challenges the main argument's assumption that tangible bodies always occupy the sense.
2
Follow the first part of the answer
The author explains how the thin plate helps weak eyes: by refining the visual spirit, not by being transparent.
This shows that the plate is still a veil in a sense.
3
Understand the transparency distinction
The author addresses the celestial spheres: they are absolutely transparent with no color, unlike crystal or glass which have some color.
This explains why spheres do not veil despite thickness.
4
Grasp the refinement about the veil
The final part clarifies that the plate is a veil in general, but it can remove other obstacles for weak eyes, so their sensation with it is stronger than without.
The claim that weak eyes see better with the plate than strong eyes is not supported.

محسوساً لم تكن كثرته موجبة لشدة الإحساس بما تحته لأن الحس يشتغل به فكلما كثر كان الاشتغال به أكثر فيقل الإحساس بما وراءه ألا ترى أن تلك الصفيحة إذا غلظت جداً أو جبت لما تحتها ستراً وأن الاستعانة بالرقيقة منها إنما هي للعيون الضعيفة دون القوية بل هي حجاب لها عن رؤية ما وراءها. واعترض عليه قوله : ( لو كان جسماً محسوساً إلخ) بخلاف ما إذا كان جسماً شفافاً كالأفلاك فإنه لا يشتغل الحس به أصلاً. قوله: (إنما هي للعيون الضعيفة) بواسطة أنَّ الحجاب بالصفحة يوجب لطافة الروح البصري وصفاءه عن الكدورات واجتماعه وقوته بسبب النفوذ في تلك الصفحة لأنها ليست بحجاب وساتر لما وراءه. قوله : ( إذا غلظت جداً إلخ) إن قلت : فما وجه عدم ستر الأفلاك ما وراءها مع كمال غلظها قلت : لأنها شفاف مطلق لالوان فيها أصلاً بخلاف صفحة البلور والزجاج الشفاف فإن فيهما لوناً ما وإن كان ضعيفاً فعلى هذا لا يلزم أن يكون الأكثر ضوءاً أكثر استناراً إلا إذا كان فيه لون ما لكنه يلزم أن لا يكون كثرته موجباً لشدة الإحساس وهذا القدر يكفي في الاستدلال لولا ما أشرنا إليه سابقاً. قوله : (بل هي حجاب لها عن رؤية ما وراءها أراد أنها حجاب لها في الجملة وبالنسبة إلى إحساسها بدونها لا أنها حجاب لها بالكلية أو بالنسبة إلى إحساس العيون الضعيفة بها بأن يكون هذا أقوى من احساس العيون القوية بها إذ المتيقن أنَّ إحساس العيون القوية بدونها أقوى من إحساسها بها لأنها حجاب في الجملة وإن إحساس العيون الضعيفة بها أقوى من إحساسها بدونها، بل قد لا يكون لها إحساس بدونها لأنها وإن كانت حجاباً في الجملة إلا أنها تدفع المانع من رؤيتها بأحد الوجهين المذكورين وأما أنَّ إحساس الضعيفة بها أقوى من إحساس القوية بها فغير ظاهر.
[If it were] a tangible (محسوساً) [body], its abundance would not be a cause for intensity of sensation of what is beneath it, because the sense is occupied by it. Whenever it increases, the occupation with it increases, so sensation of what is behind it decreases. Do you not see that [when] that plate becomes very thick, it becomes a cover for what is beneath it, and that seeking help from the thin one is only for weak eyes, not strong ones; rather it is a veil for them from seeing what is behind it. And an objection was raised to him: His saying: 'If it were a tangible body, etc.' as opposed to if it were a transparent body like the celestial spheres, for the sense is not occupied by it at all. His saying: 'They are only for weak eyes' because the veil by the plate causes subtlety of the visual spirit and its purity from turbidities and its gathering and strength due to penetration in that plate, because it is not a veil and cover for what is behind it. His saying: 'If it becomes very thick, etc.' If you say: What is the reason for the celestial spheres not covering what is behind them despite their perfect thickness? I say: Because they are absolutely transparent, having no colors at all, unlike the plate of crystal and transparent glass, for they have some color, even if weak. So according to this, it is not necessary that the more light [is] more illuminated unless there is some color, but it is necessary that its abundance does not cause intensity of sensation, and this much suffices for the proof were it not for what we indicated previously. His saying: 'Rather it is a veil for them from seeing what is behind it' – he meant that it is a veil for them in general and relative to their sensation without it, not that it is an absolute veil for them, or relative to the sensation of weak eyes with it, such that this is stronger than the sensation of strong eyes with it. For it is certain that the sensation of strong eyes without it is stronger than their sensation with it, because it is a veil in general, and that the sensation of weak eyes with it is stronger than their sensation without it; indeed they may have no sensation without it, because even though it is a veil in general, it removes the obstacle to their sight in one of the two mentioned ways. As for the claim that the sensation of weak eyes with it is stronger than the sensation of strong eyes with it, that is not apparent.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against light being a tangible body, then addresses an objection. The argument states that if light were a tangible body, its increase would not intensify sensation of what is beneath it because the sense would be occupied by the light itself; rather, more light would decrease sensation. Analogous to a thick plate that covers what is beneath it, while a thin plate aids weak eyes but still is a veil. The objection is that transparent bodies like celestial spheres do not occupy the sense. The answer explains that the thin plate aids weak eyes by refining the visual spirit, and the thick plate discussion clarifies that absolute transparency (like spheres) has no color, so it does not block vision, unlike crystal or glass which have some color. The final part refines the notion of 'veil': the plate is a veil in general but can aid weak eyes by removing obstacles; the claim that weak eyes see better with the plate than strong eyes is not evident.

Key Terms
شفاف
عيون ضعيفة
حجاب
لون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transparent bodies like glass or crystal are completely non-veiling because they are transparent.
Correction: The author explains that crystal and glass have some color, so they are not absolutely transparent; only bodies with no color at all, like celestial spheres, are non-veiling.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do celestial spheres not cover what is behind them despite their thickness?
A) They are very thin.
B) They have no color at all.
C) They are made of crystal.
D) They are intangible.

Key Takeaway
A body with any color, however faint, will veil vision when abundant; only absolute transparency avoids this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you consider 'absolutely transparent'—so obvious you never question it. What if, like the celestial spheres, its very purity makes it invisible, and you need to pay attention to the 'weak eyes' in you that cannot see it without a colored plate? What colored plates have you added to make it visible, and do they actually distort it?
?
Sanusi says the thin plate helps weak eyes by refining the visual spirit. Where in your spiritual life are you using external 'plates' (rituals, teachers, texts) to see what you cannot see on your own? Are you aware that these plates are not the sight itself, and that your goal is to strengthen your vision so that you can see without them? What step can you take today to move from weak eyes to strong eyes?

Second Argument: Light's Motion Must Be to One Direction
Second argument against light as body with objection and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against light being a body based on its natural motion being unidirectional, and how the objection and refutation unfold.

Core Idea
If light were a body, it would move naturally to one direction—but it comes from all directions.

This lesson presents the second argument from the kalam tradition against the corporeality of light. The argument exploits the principle that bodies have natural motion to a single direction, while light is observed radiating in all directions. The passage also includes an objection and its refutation, deepening the understanding of how kalam arguments are structured and defended.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the main argument: if light were a body, its motion would be natural and thus to one direction, but light emanates from all directions.
Notice the structure: premise, conclusion, contradiction.
2
Examine the objection
The objection suggests light could be multiple bodies of different natures, each moving to different directions, thus avoiding the contradiction.
This challenges the assumption that only one body emanates from a single source.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation dismisses the idea of multiple bodies from a single source as absurd, and the commentary addresses the deeper issue of proving absence of compulsion.
The discussion shifts to whether natural motion can be assumed without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان الضوء جسماً لكان حركته بالطبع إذ لا إرادة له قطعا ولا قاسر يقسره أيضاً فكانت حركته الطبيعية إلى جهة واحدة فلم يقع الضوء من كل جهة بل من جهة واحدة فقط. والتالي باطل لأن الضوء يقع على الأجسام من جهات متعددة مختلفة. واعترض عليه قوله : ( بجواز أن يكون إلخ) لاخفاء في أنَّ الكلام في وقوع الضوء من مضيء واحد والتزام انفصال أجسام مختلفة الطبائع من جسم واحد بالطبع مما لا يجترئ عليه عاقل . قوله : (جسماً) أي جسماً متحركاً ينفصل من المضيء. قوله : (إذ لا إرادة إلخ) يعني أنَّ انتفاء الإرادة والقسر معلوم بالضرورة فإن المصباح المضيء للبيت ليس فيه إرادة ولا قاسر يوجب انفصال شيء عنه، ولأن الحركة الإرادية والقسرية تختلف بحسب اختلاف الإرادة والقسر شدة وضعفاً وليس حال الضوء كذلك. قوله : ( كانت حركته الطبيعية إلخ ) لأنَّ الحيز الطبيعي لكل جسم واحد . قوله : ( والتالي باطل قال القطب في حواشي حكمة العين: لا نسلم أنَّ حركة الضوء بالطبع ليست إلى جهة واحدة إذ وقوع الضوء من كل جهة يجوز أن يكون بالقسر وكان قول الشارح ولا قاسر معه يقسره إشارة إلى دفعه لكن الكلام في إثبات انتفاء القاسر فإن عدم العلم ليس علماً بالعدم .
The second: If light were a body, its motion would be natural, since it certainly has no will nor a compeller forcing it. Then its natural motion would be to one direction, so light would not occur from every direction, but only from one direction. The consequent is false because light falls on bodies from multiple different directions. An objection was raised to him: His saying: 'It is possible that it be etc.' It is not hidden that the discussion is about light emanating from a single luminous source, and positing the separation of bodies of different natures from one body naturally is something no rational person would venture. His saying: 'a body' i.e., a moving body that separates from the luminous source. His saying: 'Since there is no will, etc.' means that the absence of will and compulsion is known by necessity, for the lamp lighting the house has no will nor a compeller causing something to separate from it, and because voluntary and compulsory motion differ according to the difference of will and compulsion in intensity and weakness, and the case of light is not like that. His saying: 'Its natural motion, etc.' because the natural place for each body is one. His saying: 'The consequent is false' – al-Qutb said in the glosses of Hikmat al-Ayn: We do not grant that the motion of light is natural and not to one direction, for the occurrence of light from every direction could be by compulsion, and the commentator's statement 'and no compeller with it compels it' is an indication to repudiate that, but the discussion is about proving the absence of the compeller, for lack of knowledge is not knowledge of absence.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against light being a body, followed by an objection and its refutation. The argument: if light were a body, it would have natural motion (since it has no will or external compeller), natural motion is to a single direction, but light comes from all directions—contradiction. The objection suggests light could be multiple bodies with different natures each moving to different directions. The refutation states that positing multiple bodies of different natures emanating from a single source is absurd. The commentary then explains key terms: 'a body' means a moving body separating from the luminous source; 'no will' is self-evident from the example of a lamp; natural motion is to one place because each body has one natural place. The commentary from al-Qutb challenges the premise that motion is natural and not to one direction, suggesting that light from all directions could be by compulsion, but the reply is that absence of a compeller is claimed, not proven.

Key Terms
جسم
حركة طبيعية
جهة واحدة
إرادة
قسر
مضيء
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes natural motion is always to one direction without considering possible compulsion (qasr) causing multi-directional motion.
Correction: The argument claims that light has no compeller (qasr), but the objection and al-Qutb's gloss point out that this absence is not proven—lack of evidence is not evidence of absence, so the natural-motion premise remains questionable.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that light cannot be a body based on its motion?
A) Light moves by will, unlike bodies.
B) Light would have natural motion to one direction, but it comes from all directions.
C) Light is not visible as a body.
D) Light does not have a natural place.

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses the principle of natural motion to one direction to disprove light being a body, but the discussion also reveals deeper issues about assuming natural motion without proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume many things about the physical world without rigorous proof—like the nature of light. What is one belief about your own faith or practice that you hold without having examined it as carefully as these scholars examine light? Are you willing to test it today?
?
The argument depends on the absence of will and compeller in light. In your own life, do you act more by will (choice) or by compulsion (habit, society, desires)? How can you ensure your religious commitments are driven by genuine will, not just compulsion?
?
If you were to apply the same logical rigor to your spiritual beliefs as the author applies to light, which assumption would be hardest to defend? What would it cost you to let it go if it were false?

Supporting Arguments from Light and Sunrise
Two supporting arguments for light not being a body, used as strengtheners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two supporting arguments that show light is not a body, and why these arguments are considered strengtheners rather than independent proofs.

Core Idea
Light's behavior is improbable for a body, but not impossible.

Sanusi presents two observations about light that suggest it is not a body. The first involves light entering a room and being blocked, the second involves sunrise. These are intended to strengthen the main argument, not to serve as conclusive proofs, because they rely on improbability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two examples
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the two distinct examples: the window example and the sunrise example.
2
Understand why each example suggests light is not a body
In the window example, light does not exit and its essence remains while its quality changes. In the sunrise example, illumination is instantaneous with no discernible motion. Consider why these are problematic if light were a body.
3
Recognize the role of these arguments as strengtheners
The author explicitly says these are not independent proofs but strengtheners, because they rely on improbability, not impossibility. Understand the difference.
This connects to the overall methodology: improbability is not certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يقوي ذلك أن النور إذا دخل في البيت من الكوة ثم سددناها دفعة واحدة فإنه لا يخرج من البيت لا قبل السد ولا بعده وهو ظاهر . ولا تعدم ذاته وإلا لزم أن تكون حيلولة الجسم بين جسمين لأحدهما ولا يبقى أيضاً على حاله الذي كان عليه بل تعدم كيفيته التي كانت مبصرة وهو مرادنا فإن تلك الكيفية الحاصلة من مقابلة المضيء الزائلة بزوالها هي الضوء، وإذا ثبت ذلك في بعض الأجسام ثبت في الكل للقطع بعدم التفاوت . وأيضا فالشمس إذا طلعت من الأفق استنارت الدنيا أي وجه الأرض وما يتصل بها في اللحظة وحركته أي حركة النور الفائض على الدنيا من الفلك الرابع إلى وجه الأرض لا تعقل فيها أي في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة ولما كانت هذه الحركة عند من يجوز خرق الأفلاك غير مستحيلة بل مستبعدة كاستبعاد انتفاء الجسم بالحيلولة بينه وبين غيره جعل هذين الوجهين مقويين لما تقدم لا دليلين مستقلين لأن الاستبعاد لا يكون دليلاً على ما يطلب فيه اليقين . احتج الخصم على كون الضوء جسماً بأن الضوء متحرك لأنه منحدر عن المضيء العالي كالشمس والنار وكل منحدر متحرك ويتبعه في الحركة أي يتحرك بحركته كما في الشمس . قوله : ( أي يتحرك بحركته أي بسبب حركته فحركة الضوء ذاتية فلا يرد أن الحركة بالتبع لا تقتضي أن يكون المتحرك جسماً . قوله : ( ولا تعدم ذاته وإلا إلخ قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يشترط وجود بعض الأجسام بمقابلة المضيء كالشمس أو ينقلب هواء عند عدمها كالنار عند ما حال شيء بين أجزائها الممتدة على المصباح أو يكون الضوء جسماً مكيفاً يشترط رؤيته لكيفيته فيزول فلا نرى؟ وقوله : وهو مرادنا ممنوع وأنت خبير بما سيصرح الآن من أن المدعي الاستبعاد لا عدم الجواز كما دل عليه جعله مقوياً لا دليلاً فبهذا يندفع بعض هذه الوجوه كما لا يخفى .
And what strengthens that is that when light enters the house through the window then we block it suddenly, it does not exit from the house neither before the blocking nor after, and that is apparent. And its essence does not cease to exist, otherwise the interposition of a body between two bodies would entail [its cessation] for one of them, nor does it remain in the state it was in, rather its quality which was sight-giving ceases to exist, and that is our intention. For that quality obtained from facing the luminous source, which ceases with its cessation, is light. And if this is established in some bodies, it is established in all, due to certainty of no difference. Also, when the sun rises from the horizon, the world (i.e., the face of the earth and what is connected to it) becomes illuminated in an instant, and its motion (i.e., the motion of the light flowing over the world from the fourth sphere to the face of the earth) is not conceivable in that (i.e., in that subtle instant). And since this motion, according to one who permits the piercing of the spheres, is not impossible but improbable, like the improbability of the annihilation of a body by interposition between it and another, he made these two aspects strengtheners for what preceded, not independent proofs, because improbability is not proof for what requires certainty. The opponent argued that light is a body because light is moving, since it descends from the high luminous source like the sun and fire, and everything that descends is moving, and it follows it in motion, i.e., it moves by its motion as with the sun. His saying: 'i.e., it moves by its motion, meaning due to its motion, so the motion of light is intrinsic, so it is not objected that motion by following does not require the moving thing to be a body.' His saying: 'And its essence does not cease, etc.' It was said: Why is it not permissible that the existence of some bodies is conditioned on facing the luminous source like the sun, or that air turns into fire when it is absent? Or when something intervenes between its parts extending over the lamp, or that light is a qualified body whose visibility is conditioned by its quality, so it ceases and we do not see? His saying: 'And that is our intention' is denied. And you are aware that what will be stated now is that the claim is improbability, not impossibility of occurrence, as indicated by his making it a strengthener not a proof. Thus some of these aspects are repelled, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides two supporting arguments for the claim that light is not a body, then presents and refutes an opponent's proof. The first supporting argument: light entering through a window and then being blocked does not exit and does not cease to exist as a substance; rather its quality (being sight-giving) ceases. This quality is light itself. The second: sunlight instantly illuminates the earth, with no discernible motion. These are not used as independent proofs but as strengtheners because they rely on improbability rather than impossibility. The opponent argues that light is a body because it moves (descends from a source and follows its motion). The response explains that 'follows in motion' means motion is intrinsic, not that it moves by following some- thing else; thus it does not prove corporeality. Additional objections about the first argument are raised (e.g., light might be conditioned on facing the source, etc.) and dismissed by noting the author's claim is improbability, not impossibility.

كيفية

شمس

استبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These observations are meant as definitive proofs that light is not a body.
Correction: The author explicitly states that they are strengtheners based on improbability, not conclusive proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author call the arguments from the window and sunrise 'strengtheners' and not independent proofs?
A) Because they are not based on logical necessity.
B) Because they rely on improbability, not impossibility.
C) Because they only apply to certain types of light.
D) Because they were borrowed from other philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The two examples show that light behaves in ways that are improbable for a body, but the author uses them only as strengtheners, not as definitive proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the author's careful distinction between 'strengthener' and 'proof', does your own heart have any cherished belief that you treat as certain but that really only has probable evidence? Will you examine it honestly today?
?
The light's essence remained while its quality vanished—what permanent identity do you assume about yourself that is actually just a transient quality? Are you ready to let it go when the Source of that quality is blocked?
?
The sunrise instantaneously illuminates the world without perceptible motion—when was the last time you stood in awe of such a 'mundane' miracle? Or do you merely assume it's 'natural' and so miss the Creator behind it? Commit to one moment of reflection tomorrow at dawn.

Refutation of the Opponent's Proof That Light Is a Body
Opponent's proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that light is a body because it moves, and why this argument fails.

Core Idea
Motion alone does not prove corporeality; light's motion is intrinsic.

The opponent attempts to prove that light is a body by pointing out that light moves: it descends from the sun and follows its motion. The author responds by clarifying that light's motion is intrinsic and does not require it to be a body. Additionally, objections to the earlier window argument are addressed, affirming that the claim is one of improbability.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
The opponent argues: light moves (descends from source, follows its motion), therefore it is a body. Identify the logical structure.
2
Understand the author's response
The author counters by explaining that the motion of light is intrinsic, not like the motion of a body that pushes or pulls. Thus the opponent's inference is invalid.
3
See how objections are deflected
The author addresses potential objections to the earlier window argument by reiterating that the claim is about improbability, not impossibility.
This reinforces the methodological point.

ومما يقوي ذلك أن النور إذا دخل في البيت من الكوة ثم سددناها دفعة واحدة فإنه لا يخرج من البيت لا قبل السد ولا بعده وهو ظاهر . ولا تعدم ذاته وإلا لزم أن تكون حيلولة الجسم بين جسمين لأحدهما ولا يبقى أيضاً على حاله الذي كان عليه بل تعدم كيفيته التي كانت مبصرة وهو مرادنا فإن تلك الكيفية الحاصلة من مقابلة المضيء الزائلة بزوالها هي الضوء، وإذا ثبت ذلك في بعض الأجسام ثبت في الكل للقطع بعدم التفاوت . وأيضا فالشمس إذا طلعت من الأفق استنارت الدنيا أي وجه الأرض وما يتصل بها في اللحظة وحركته أي حركة النور الفائض على الدنيا من الفلك الرابع إلى وجه الأرض لا تعقل فيها أي في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة ولما كانت هذه الحركة عند من يجوز خرق الأفلاك غير مستحيلة بل مستبعدة كاستبعاد انتفاء الجسم بالحيلولة بينه وبين غيره جعل هذين الوجهين مقويين لما تقدم لا دليلين مستقلين لأن الاستبعاد لا يكون دليلاً على ما يطلب فيه اليقين . احتج الخصم على كون الضوء جسماً بأن الضوء متحرك لأنه منحدر عن المضيء العالي كالشمس والنار وكل منحدر متحرك ويتبعه في الحركة أي يتحرك بحركته كما في الشمس . قوله : ( أي يتحرك بحركته أي بسبب حركته فحركة الضوء ذاتية فلا يرد أن الحركة بالتبع لا تقتضي أن يكون المتحرك جسماً . قوله : ( ولا تعدم ذاته وإلا إلخ قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يشترط وجود بعض الأجسام بمقابلة المضيء كالشمس أو ينقلب هواء عند عدمها كالنار عند ما حال شيء بين أجزائها الممتدة على المصباح أو يكون الضوء جسماً مكيفاً يشترط رؤيته لكيفيته فيزول فلا نرى؟ وقوله : وهو مرادنا ممنوع وأنت خبير بما سيصرح الآن من أن المدعي الاستبعاد لا عدم الجواز كما دل عليه جعله مقوياً لا دليلاً فبهذا يندفع بعض هذه الوجوه كما لا يخفى .
And what strengthens that is that when light enters the house through the window then we block it suddenly, it does not exit from the house neither before the blocking nor after, and that is apparent. And its essence does not cease to exist, otherwise the interposition of a body between two bodies would entail [its cessation] for one of them, nor does it remain in the state it was in, rather its quality which was sight-giving ceases to exist, and that is our intention. For that quality obtained from facing the luminous source, which ceases with its cessation, is light. And if this is established in some bodies, it is established in all, due to certainty of no difference. Also, when the sun rises from the horizon, the world (i.e., the face of the earth and what is connected to it) becomes illuminated in an instant, and its motion (i.e., the motion of the light flowing over the world from the fourth sphere to the face of the earth) is not conceivable in that (i.e., in that subtle instant). And since this motion, according to one who permits the piercing of the spheres, is not impossible but improbable, like the improbability of the annihilation of a body by interposition between it and another, he made these two aspects strengtheners for what preceded, not independent proofs, because improbability is not proof for what requires certainty. The opponent argued that light is a body because light is moving, since it descends from the high luminous source like the sun and fire, and everything that descends is moving, and it follows it in motion, i.e., it moves by its motion as with the sun. His saying: 'i.e., it moves by its motion, meaning due to its motion, so the motion of light is intrinsic, so it is not objected that motion by following does not require the moving thing to be a body.' His saying: 'And its essence does not cease, etc.' It was said: Why is it not permissible that the existence of some bodies is conditioned on facing the luminous source like the sun, or that air turns into fire when it is absent? Or when something intervenes between its parts extending over the lamp, or that light is a qualified body whose visibility is conditioned by its quality, so it ceases and we do not see? His saying: 'And that is our intention' is denied. And you are aware that what will be stated now is that the claim is improbability, not impossibility of occurrence, as indicated by his making it a strengthener not a proof. Thus some of these aspects are repelled, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides two supporting arguments for the claim that light is not a body, then presents and refutes an opponent's proof. The first supporting argument: light entering through a window and then being blocked does not exit and does not cease to exist as a substance; rather its quality (being sight-giving) ceases. This quality is light itself. The second: sunlight instantly illuminates the earth, with no discernible motion. These are not used as independent proofs but as strengtheners because they rely on improbability rather than impossibility. The opponent argues that light is a body because it moves (descends from a source and follows its motion). The response explains that 'follows in motion' means motion is intrinsic, not that it moves by following some- thing else; thus it does not prove corporeality. Additional objections about the first argument are raised (e.g., light might be conditioned on facing the source, etc.) and dismissed by noting the author's claim is improbability, not impossibility.

Key Terms
جسم
حركة
ذاتي
استبعاد
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because light moves, it must be a body.
Correction: Motion does not necessarily indicate corporeality; light's motion is intrinsic, not like the motion of a body.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the opponent's claim that light moves like a body?
A) By denying that light moves.
B) By saying the motion of light is intrinsic, not like the motion of a body.
C) By saying light is not a body because it does not move.
D) By saying the opponent is not an expert.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument fails because light's motion is intrinsic and does not imply corporeality; the author's stance is based on improbability, not impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems convincing on the surface, do you stop to examine whether its premises are sound, or do you accept it without scrutiny? How might this lesson's subtle refutation change your approach to evaluating claims about your faith?
?
The author claims improbability, not impossibility. Is there an area in your life where you have assumed something is impossible, while it is only improbable with Allah? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your assumptions?
?
Light, which you see every day, is not a body in this theological account. What does this teach you about the limits of human reason in grasping the true nature of Allah's creation? Have you ever been too confident in your own reasoning about religious matters, and what will you do about that now?

The Weak Argument: Colors Sharing in Degrees
The commentator presents and dismisses a weak argument about light and color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that light is merely the appearance of color is weak, based on the argument that colors of different realities cannot share in a single essence.

Core Idea
Light in white and black cannot be identical because they differ in essence.

The commentator begins by presenting the philosophers' view that light is the appearance of color, and then offers a weak argument that colors share in having degrees of appearance. He quickly dismisses this as very weak, pointing out that light in white and light in black are similar in essence but the colors themselves are not, showing that light must be an additional quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to understand the flow.
2
Identify the weak argument
Notice the opponent's claim that colors of different realities share in having degrees of appearance.
The commentator calls this 'very weak'.
3
Understand why it is weak
The passage argues that light in white and black is similar but the colors differ, so light must be additional.
This sets up the need for a stronger refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد المرصد الثالث - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في الضوء ظهور اللون معنى ( وأنه ( عطف على إذ ربما أي ولأنه ( يجوز اشتراك ) الأمور المتخالفة بالماهية في أمر ذاتي أو عرضي، فيجوز حينئذ اشتراك ( الألوان ) المختلفة الحقائق ( في كونها ذات مراتب ) أي في الظهور الذي له مراتب متفاوتة وهذا ضعيف جداً إذ أنَّ الضوء الذي في البياض يماثل في الماهية الضوء الذي في السواد كما يشهد به الحس، وهما لا يتماثلان في الماهية قطعاً فلا يكون ضوء كل منهما عينه، بل أمراً زائداً عليه وإذ قد بطل هذان الوجهان ( فالمعتمد ( في الرد على هذا القائل. ( أنَّ البلور في الظلمة إذا وقع عليه ضوء يرى ضوؤه ولا يرى لونه لعدمه فقد وجد الضوء بدون اللون كما قد وجد أيضاً اللون بدونه، فإن السواد وغيره من الألوان قد لا يكون مضيئاً، وأيضاً لو كان الضوء عين اللون لكان بعضه ضداً لبعضه لكنه باطل لأنَّ الضوء لا يقابله إلا الظلمة احتج) القائل بأنَّ الضوء هو ظهور اللون لا كيفية زائدة عليه بل الحس كما مر إذا ترقى من الأدنى إلى الأعلى ظن هناك بريقاً ولمعاناً ( بأنه يزول ) الضوء الأضعف بالأقوى كاللامع (بالليل مثل اليراعة وعين الهرة فإنه مضيئاً یری في الظلمة ولا يرى ضوء في السراج ثم السراج) فإنه يرى مضيئاً شديداً ويضمحل ضوؤه في ضوء القمر (ثم القمر فإنه مضيء ولا ضوء له في الشمس ) ثم الشمس ) فإنها الغابة في الإضاءة التي يزول فيها ضوء ما عداها ( وما هو) أي ليس زوال الأضعف بالأقوى (إلا) لأن الحس لا يدرك الأضعف عند الأقوى ولا زوال ثمة ) بحسب نفس الأمر، بل الحس لما ضعف في الظلمة وكان للامع بالليل قدر من الظهور ظن أن ذلك الظهور كيفية زائدة على لونه ثم إذا تقوى بنور السراج ونظر إلى اللامع لم ير له لمعاناً لزوال ضعف البصر وكذا الكلام في السراج والقمر فقد ظهر أن أضواء هذه الأشياء ليست إلا ظهور ألوانها عند الحس كما أنَّ زوالها ليس إلا خفاء ألوانها عنده فلا يكون الضوء كيفية زائدة على اللون وظهوره (قلنا : هذا تمثيل أي إيراد مثال غايته تجويز أن يكون لذلك الذي ذكرتموه (آثر) في اختلاف أحوال الإدراكات في قوتها وضعفها بحسب اختلاف الحس في قوته وضعفه، ولا يدل على أنَّ الضوء ليس كيفية موجودة زائدة على اللون وظهوره إذ قد مر أن الحس لا ينفعل عن الأضعف الموجود في نفسه عند انفعاله عن الأقوى فيجوز أن يكون للامع مثلاً ضوء مغاير للونه إلا أنه لا يرى في ضوء السراج .
The intended meaning. The third observation - the second section - the first objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning light as the appearance of color. (And that) is connected to 'since perhaps', i.e., because it is permissible for things differing in essence to share in a essential or accidental matter, so it is permissible then that colors of different realities share in having degrees, i.e., in the appearance which has varying degrees. This is very weak, because the light in whiteness is similar in essence to the light in blackness, as sense witnesses, and they are definitely not similar in essence, so the light of each is not identical to it, but rather an additional thing. And since these two aspects have been invalidated, the relied-upon [argument] in refuting this proponent is that a crystal in darkness, when light falls on it, its light is seen and its color is not seen because it is absent; so light has been found without color, just as color has also been found without it. For blackness and other colors may not be luminous. Also, if light were identical to color, then some of it would be opposite to some of it, but that is false because light is only opposed by darkness. The proponent that light is the appearance of color and not an additional quality, but rather sense, as previously, when it ascends from the lower to the higher, supposes there a sparkle and gleam, argued that the weaker light is removed by the stronger, like the luminous (at night such as the firefly and the cat's eye—it is seen luminous in darkness and no light is seen in a lamp—then the lamp is seen intensely luminous and its light disappears in moonlight, then the moon is luminous and has no light in sunlight, then the sun is the extreme in illumination in which the light of everything else disappears). And this removal of the weaker by the stronger is only because sense does not perceive the weaker when the stronger is present, and there is no removal in actuality. Rather, since sense was weak in darkness and the nocturnal luminous had a degree of appearance, it supposed that appearance to be an additional quality on its color; then when it is strengthened by the light of the lamp and looks at the luminous, it sees no gleam for it due to the removal of the weakness of sight. The same applies to the lamp and the moon. So it is evident that the lights of these things are nothing but appearances of their colors to sense, just as their disappearance is nothing but the concealment of their colors from it. Thus light is not an additional quality beyond color and its appearance. We said: This is an example, i.e., adducing an instance whose purpose is to allow that what you mentioned has an effect in the varying states of perceptions in their strength and weakness according to the variation of sense in its strength and weakness, and it does not indicate that light is not an existing quality additional to color and its appearance, because it has passed that sense is not affected by the weaker existing in itself when it is affected by the stronger. So it is possible that the luminous, for example, has a light different from its color, except that it is not seen in the light of the lamp.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that light is merely the appearance of color and not an additional quality. It opens by presenting the proponents' argument that different colors share in having degrees of appearance, which the commentator immediately dismisses as very weak, noting that light in white and black are not identical in essence—sense shows they differ, so light must be an extra quality. Then the commentator gives the relied-upon refutation: first, a crystal in darkness shows light without color (its own color is absent), and colors exist without light (e.g., black in shadow); second, if light were identical to color, then lights would oppose each other like colors do, but light is only opposed by darkness. Next, an objection is presented: the opponent argues that the gradual disappearance of weaker lights in stronger ones (e.g., firefly, lamp, moon, sun) shows that these are not separate lights but mere appearances of colors perceived more or less intensely by sense. The commentator answers that this is merely an example showing that perception varies with sensory strength, not proof that light is not an extra reality. The sense is overwhelmed by the stronger stimulus and fails to perceive the weaker existing quality; hence, it is possible that the firefly has a real light distinct from its color, but it is not seen in the lamp's stronger light.

Key Terms
ضوء (light)
لون (color)
ظهور (appearance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Colors sharing in degrees of appearance proves that light is merely the appearance of color.
Correction: Light is similar across different colors while the colors themselves differ in essence, so light cannot be identical to color but must be an additional quality.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the argument that colors share in degrees of appearance very weak?
A) Because light in white and black are similar but the colors differ, showing light is additional.
B) Because light cannot be seen.
C) Because colors are not real.
D) Because the philosophers disagree.

Key Takeaway
The weak argument fails because light is similar across colors while the colors themselves differ, proving light is an additional quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator dismisses an argument as 'very weak' because it fails the test of sense perception. Think of an area in your life where you have been holding onto a comfortable belief or justification that you have never tested against reality. Are you willing to subject it to the same scrutiny? What would it cost you to admit it is weak?
?
The weak argument sounds plausible: different colors share in degrees of appearance, so light must be identical to appearance. How many times have you accepted a plausible-sounding claim because it was convenient or because you wanted it to be true? Name one such claim you believed without evidence. Will you now seek the truth even if it unsettles you?
?
The passage shows that similar appearances can mask deep differences in essence. In your relationship with Allah, are you mistaking outward similarity of actions (e.g., praying, fasting) with the inner reality of sincerity and devotion? What would it mean for you to stop assuming your deeds are enough and instead examine the state of your heart today?

Causality and Created Things

The Refutation: Light Is Not Identical to Color
The commentator gives two arguments for light being additional to color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relied-upon refutation that light is not identical to color, using the example of a crystal and the argument from opposition.

Core Idea
Light can exist without color and color without light; they are not the same.

After dismissing the weak argument, the commentator presents the stronger refutation. Two key points: first, a crystal in darkness shows light without its own color, and colors like black can exist without being luminous. Second, if light were identical to color, then lights would oppose each other like colors do, but light is only opposed by darkness. These prove light is an additional quality.

Guided Path
1
Examine the crystal example
Read how a crystal in darkness when light falls on it shows light without its color.
The crystal's own color is unseen because it is absent.
2
Consider color without light
Notice that blackness and other colors may not be luminous, showing color without light.
3
Understand the opposition argument
If light were identical to color, then lights would be opposites, but light is only opposed by darkness.
This shows light and color are not the same.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that light is merely the appearance of color and not an additional quality. It opens by presenting the proponents' argument that different colors share in having degrees of appearance, which the commentator immediately dismisses as very weak, noting that light in white and black are not identical in essence—sense shows they differ, so light must be an extra quality. Then the commentator gives the relied-upon refutation: first, a crystal in darkness shows light without color (its own color is absent), and colors exist without light (e.g., black in shadow); second, if light were identical to color, then lights would oppose each other like colors do, but light is only opposed by darkness. Next, an objection is presented: the opponent argues that the gradual disappearance of weaker lights in stronger ones (e.g., firefly, lamp, moon, sun) shows that these are not separate lights but mere appearances of colors perceived more or less intensely by sense. The commentator answers that this is merely an example showing that perception varies with sensory strength, not proof that light is not an extra reality. The sense is overwhelmed by the stronger stimulus and fails to perceive the weaker existing quality; hence, it is possible that the firefly has a real light distinct from its color, but it is not seen in the lamp's stronger light.

Key Terms
ضوء (light)
لون (color)
بلور (crystal)
ظلمة (darkness)
ضد (opposite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light and color are identical because they are often perceived together.
Correction: They can exist separately: light without color (crystal) and color without light (blackness).

Checkpoint
What does the example of a crystal in darkness prove?
A) Light can exist without color.
B) Color can exist without light.
C) Light and color are identical.
D) The crystal has no color.

Key Takeaway
Light and color are separate because each can exist without the other, and light does not have opposites like colors do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The crystal example shows that light can be seen even when color is hidden. In your life, are you more concerned with the 'color' of your actions (how they appear to others) than with the 'light' of sincerity that only Allah sees? What one step can you take today to prioritize inner light over outer appearance?
?
The passage argues that light has no opposite except darkness. Do you treat other believers who differ from you in practice as opponents, forgetting that the only real opposition is disbelief? Will you commit to seeing the common light of faith in every Muslim, even when their 'color' differs?
?
If light were identical to color, confusion would arise. Have you ever conflated two separate things in your relationship with Allah—like mixing love for Him with love for worldly pleasures? Take a moment to identify one such confusion and resolve to distinguish them from now on.

Answering the Objection of Gradual Diminishment
The commentator refutes the objection based on the gradation of light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that the gradual disappearance of weaker lights in stronger ones shows that light is merely an appearance of color.

Core Idea
Sensory limitation, not ontological non-existence, explains the disappearance of light.

The opponent argues that the progression from firefly to lamp to moon to sun shows that weaker lights disappear in stronger ones, suggesting that these are not real lights but merely appearances of color. The commentator answers that this is due to the sense's inability to perceive the weaker when occupied by the stronger, not an actual removal of light. Thus, the firefly may have a real light that is simply not seen in the lamp's stronger light.

Guided Path
1
Review the objection
Read the opponent's gradation argument: firefly, lamp, moon, sun each making the previous light disappear.
The opponent claims this shows light is just appearance.
2
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator explains that sense does not perceive the weaker when the stronger is present, but no actual removal occurs.
The weaker light still exists in reality.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The objection does not prove light is not an extra quality; it only shows sensory limitation.
The firefly may have a real light unseen under the lamp.

The intended meaning. The third observation - the second section - the first objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning light as the appearance of color. (And that) is connected to 'since perhaps', i.e., because it is permissible for things differing in essence to share in a essential or accidental matter, so it is permissible then that colors of different realities share in having degrees, i.e., in the appearance which has varying degrees. This is very weak, because the light in whiteness is similar in essence to the light in blackness, as sense witnesses, and they are definitely not similar in essence, so the light of each is not identical to it, but rather an additional thing. And since these two aspects have been invalidated, the relied-upon [argument] in refuting this proponent is that a crystal in darkness, when light falls on it, its light is seen and its color is not seen because it is absent; so light has been found without color, just as color has also been found without it. For blackness and other colors may not be luminous. Also, if light were identical to color, then some of it would be opposite to some of it, but that is false because light is only opposed by darkness. The proponent that light is the appearance of color and not an additional quality, but rather sense, as previously, when it ascends from the lower to the higher, supposes there a sparkle and gleam, argued that the weaker light is removed by the stronger, like the luminous (at night such as the firefly and the cat's eye—it is seen luminous in darkness and no light is seen in a lamp—then the lamp is seen intensely luminous and its light disappears in moonlight, then the moon is luminous and has no light in sunlight, then the sun is the extreme in illumination in which the light of everything else disappears). And this removal of the weaker by the stronger is only because sense does not perceive the weaker when the stronger is present, and there is no removal in actuality. Rather, since sense was weak in darkness and the nocturnal luminous had a degree of appearance, it supposed that appearance to be an additional quality on its color; then when it is strengthened by the light of the lamp and looks at the luminous, it sees no gleam for it due to the removal of the weakness of sight. The same applies to the lamp and the moon. So it is evident that the lights of these things are nothing but appearances of their colors to sense, just as their disappearance is nothing but the concealment of their colors from it. Thus light is not an additional quality beyond color and its appearance. We said: This is an example, i.e., adducing an instance whose purpose is to allow that what you mentioned has an effect in the varying states of perceptions in their strength and weakness according to the variation of sense in its strength and weakness, and it does not indicate that light is not an existing quality additional to color and its appearance, because it has passed that sense is not affected by the weaker existing in itself when it is affected by the stronger. So it is possible that the luminous, for example, has a light different from its color, except that it is not seen in the light of the lamp.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that light is merely the appearance of color and not an additional quality. The commentator answers that this is merely an example showing that perception varies with sensory strength, not proof that light is not an extra reality. The Circularity of Light and Color
Denial of air's illumination and resolution of circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that air cannot receive light because it lacks color, and how the charge of circularity between light and color is resolved through the concept of circularity of simultaneity.

Core Idea
Light and color are mutually dependent, but circularity of simultaneity is permissible.

In this passage, the commentator presents the first major objection to the idea that air is modified by light: since air is colorless and color is a prerequisite for receiving light, air cannot be illuminated. But this raises a circularity: if light is necessary for color, how can color be necessary for light? The answer introduces a key logical concept in kalam: circularity of simultaneity (دور معية), which is not vicious.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial argument
Examine the claim that air cannot receive light because it has no color, which is a necessary condition for modification by light.
Notice that the denial is based on the simplicity of air.
2
Understand the circularity objection
An objector points out that if light is a condition for color (as per philosophers), then making color a condition for light creates a circle. Identify this logical challenge.
This is a standard criticism of the denial.
3
Grasp the resolution: circularity of simultaneity
The response states that the circle is a 'circularity of simultaneity' (دور معية), which is not logically invalid. Reflect on why this type of circle is acceptable.
Key insight: mutually necessary conditions can coexist without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : هل يتكيف الهواء بالضوء ] ( هل يتكيف الهواء بالضوء ) أو لا ؟ وإنما أورده هاهنا لأن ما ذكره في المقصد الثاني من مراتب الظل متوقف على تكييف الهواء بالضوء (منهم من منعه وجعل شرطه أي شرط التكيف بالضوء (اللون ) ولا لون للهواء لكونه بسيطاً، فلا يقبل الضوء لانتفاء شرطه، ولما كان لقائل أن يقول : قد مر أن الضوء شرط لوجود اللون عند الحكيم فلو كان اللون شرطاً للضوء أيضاً لدار. أجاب عنه بقوله: (فكل) من الضوء واللون ) شرط للآخر والدور دور معينة فلا امتناع فيه لما عرفت من جواز امتناع من الجانبين ( ويبطله ) أي يبطل قول المانع ( أنا نرى في الصبح الأفق مضيئاً وما هو إلا الهواء تكيف بالضوء، وقد يجاب عنه بأن ذلك للأجزاء البخارية المختلطة به أي بالهواء ) والكلام في الهواء الصرف ( الخالي عن الأجزاء الدخانية والهبائية والبخارية القابلة للضوء بسبب كونها متلونة في الجملة ورده الإمام الرازي بأنه يلزم من ذلك الهواء، كلما كان أصفى كان الضوء الحاصل فيه قبل الطلوع وبعد الغروب وفي أفنية الجدران ،أضعف وكلما كان البخار والغبار فيه أكثر كان ضوؤه أقوى لكن الأمر بالعكس، واحتج على استضاءة الهواء بوجه آخر أيضاً، هو أنه لو لم يتكيف الهواء بالضوء لوجب أن يرى بالنهار الكواكب التي في خلاف جهة الشمس، لأنَّ الكواكب باقية على ضوئها والحس لم ينفعل على ذلك التقدير من ضوء أقوى
The third goal: whether air is modified by light. (Is air modified by light) or not? He only introduced it here because what he mentioned in the second goal about the ranks of shadow depends on the modification of air by light. Some denied it and made its condition (i.e., the condition of modification by light) color, and air has no color because it is simple, so it does not receive light due to the absence of its condition. And since a speaker could say: it has passed that light is a condition for the existence of color according to the philosopher, so if color were also a condition for light, there would be circularity. He answered it by his saying: (each) of light and color is a condition for the other, and the circularity is a circularity of simultaneity (دور معية), so there is no impossibility in it, because you have known the permissibility of impossibility from both sides. And it (i.e., the denial) is invalidated by what we see at dawn: the horizon is luminous, and it is nothing but air modified by light. And it may be answered that that is due to the vaporous parts mixed with it (i.e., with air), but the discussion is about pure air free from smoky, dusty, and vaporous parts that are receptive to light due to being colored in some way. And Imam al-Razi refuted it by saying that it would entail that the purer the air, the weaker the light obtained in it before sunrise and after sunset and in courtyards, and the more vapor and dust in it, the stronger its light, but the opposite is the case. And he argued for the illumination of air by another way also: that if air were not modified by light, then during the day one would necessarily see the stars in the direction opposite the sun, because the stars retain their light, and the sense would not be affected on that supposition by a stronger light.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about whether air is modified by (receives) light. The commentator first explains why this topic is placed here: because the previous discussion on shadow ranks depends on air being illuminated. He then presents the position of those who deny it: they argue that modification by light requires color, and air has no color because it is simple. An objection is raised: if light is a condition for color (as per philosophers), and color is a condition for light, that would be circular. The denial's answer is that the circularity is of simultaneity (دور معية), which is permissible. Then the commentator gives an empirical refutation: we see the horizon luminous at dawn, which is air modified by light. A counter-answer suggests that this is due to vapors, but that only applies to pure air. Al-Razi then refutes that counter-answer by noting that if vapors were the cause, then pure air would be darker, but the opposite is observed. Finally, al-Razi offers another proof: if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day because there would be no stronger light to overpower them.

Key Terms
التكييف
الضوء
اللون
دور المعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All logical circularities are invalid and must be rejected.
Correction: Circularity of simultaneity (دور معية) is a recognized exception; it does not entail logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
How is the charge of circularity between light and color resolved?
A) By denying that light is a condition for color
B) By accepting the circularity as a circularity of simultaneity
C) By rejecting the original denial outright
D) By introducing a third condition

Key Takeaway
Air's lack of color presents a challenge, but the circularity between light and color is resolved by recognizing it as a circularity of simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an apparent circularity in your understanding of Allah's attributes or His relationship with creation, do you automatically dismiss it as irrational, or are you willing to investigate whether it might be a permissible mutual dependency (دور معية)? How might this affect your trust in Islamic theology?
?
This lesson shows that even knowledgeable scholars accept some logical paradoxes as non-vicious. Is there a belief or practice in your life that seems contradictory to you, but which you might need to accept as mutually conditioning rather than rejecting outright? What would it cost you to embrace that tension?

Empirical and Rational Proofs for Air's Illumination
Empirical evidence for air's illumination and al-Razi's refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical argument from dawn, the counterargument about vaporous parts, and al-Razi's two refutations: the observational argument about air purity and the rational argument about seeing stars.

Core Idea
We see air illuminated at dawn, and al-Razi shows that the purer air is, the brighter it appears, disproving the vapor theory.

After the logical dispute, the commentator presents empirical evidence: the luminous horizon at dawn can only be explained by air being modified by light. Opponents counter that this is due to vaporous particles. Al-Razi refutes both this empirical counter and provides a second rational proof: if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day.

Guided Path
1
Observe the dawn evidence
Read the claim that the bright horizon at dawn is air modified by light. Consider why this is taken as direct proof.
This is an appeal to common observation.
2
Examine the vaporous parts counter
Understand the rebuttal: the dawn illumination is due to vapors mixed with air, not pure air itself.
This shifts the target to pure air.
3
Analyze al-Razi's observational refutation
Al-Razi argues that if vapors were the cause, purer air would be darker, but the opposite is true. Trace this reasoning.
He uses an inversion of expected outcome.
4
Understand al-Razi's star argument
Al-Razi offers a second proof: if air were not illuminated, stars would be visible during the day due to absence of overpowering light. Grasp the implication.
This is a rational deduction from the denial's premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He only introduced it here because what he mentioned in the second goal about the ranks of shadow depends on the modification of air by light. Some denied it and made its condition (i.e., the condition of modification by light) color, and air has no color because it is simple, so it does not receive light due to the absence of its condition. Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about whether air is modified by (receives) light. The commentator first explains why this topic is placed here: because the previous discussion on shadow ranks depends on air being illuminated. He then presents the position of those who deny it: they argue that modification by light requires color, and air has no color because it is simple. An objection is raised: if light is a condition for color (as per philosophers), and color is a condition for light, that would be circular. The denial's answer is that the circularity is of simultaneity (دور معية), which is permissible. Then the commentator gives an empirical refutation: we see the horizon luminous at dawn, which is air modified by light. A counter-answer suggests that this is due to vapors, but that only applies to pure air. Al-Razi then refutes that counter-answer by noting that if vapors were the cause, then pure air would be darker, but the opposite is observed. Finally, al-Razi offers another proof: if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day because there would be no stronger light to overpower them.

Key Terms
الضوء
الهواء الصرف
الأجزاء البخارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi's observational argument show about vapors?
A) They are the true cause of illumination
B) Pure air is darker than vapor-laden air
C) The purer the air, the brighter the light, contradicting the vapor theory
D) Vapors are irrelevant to the discussion

Key Takeaway
Both empirical and rational arguments confirm that air is modified by light, with al-Razi providing decisive refutations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the dawn or sunset, do you see it as a mere physical phenomenon, or does it remind you of the subtle signs of Allah's creation that require careful reflection? How often do you pause to contemplate the deeper truths behind everyday occurrences?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that impurities weaken the light. What 'impurities' in your soul—like pride, laziness, or attachment to false assumptions—are blocking the light of truth from reaching your heart? What concrete step will you take today to remove one of them?
?
If you were to apply Al-Razi's method of critical questioning to a belief you hold deeply but have never examined, which belief would that be? Are you willing to let it be tested against clear evidence, even if it means changing your mind?

Weakness in al-Razi's Refutation of the Vapor Argument
A gloss critiquing al-Razi's refutation of the vapor argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weakness in al-Razi's argument against vapor causality and the principle that excess can be as harmful as deficiency.

Core Idea
Excess can be as harmful as deficiency — al-Razi overlooked this.

In this gloss, al-Razi's refutation of the vapor argument for luminosity is examined. The commentator points out a flaw: al-Razi assumes that adding more vapor always increases light, but the true cause may require a specific mix, and too much can reduce light.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the critique.
Notice how the commentator directly quotes al-Razi before offering the weakness.
2
Understand al-Razi's refutation
Identify what al-Razi argued: that if vapor caused light, then pure air should be darker, but observation shows the opposite.
This is the argument being critiqued.
3
Grasp the weakness
The gloss says the cause may be a specific mixture; exceeding that reduces light, so both excess and deficiency harm. Thus al-Razi's refutation is not decisive.
This introduces the principle of optimal mixture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ورده الإمام الرازي قال في شرح المقاصد : فيه ضعف لجواز أن يكون الموجب مخالطة الأجزاء إلى حد مخصوص إذا تجاوز أخذ الضوء في النقصان وحاصله أنه أن يضره الإفراط كما يضره التفريط .
His statement: 'And Imam al-Razi refuted it' – he said in Sharh al-Maqasid: there is weakness in it because it is possible that the cause is the mixing of parts to a specific limit; if it exceeds, the light begins to decrease. The gist is that excess harms it just as deficiency harms it.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This gloss critiques al-Razi's refutation of the argument that the luminosity at dawn is due to vaporous parts in the air. Al-Razi had argued that if vapors caused light, then purer air would be darker, contradicting observation. The gloss notes a weakness: it is possible that mixing of parts to a specific limit causes light, and beyond that limit (excess) the light decreases. So both excess and deficiency can reduce light, which would explain why pure air (no vapor) and too much vapor both give weaker light. Thus al-Razi's argument is not decisive.

Key Terms
الأجزاء البخارية
الإفراط
التفريط
النقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding more vapor always increases light
Correction: The cause may be a specific mixture; too much vapor can decrease light as well, as with the optimal range.

Checkpoint
What is the weakness in al-Razi's refutation according to this gloss?
A) It requires an infinite regress
B) It assumes vapor always increases light, but too much vapor can decrease light
C) It rejects empirical observation
D) It is based on a false premise about color

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's refutation is not conclusive because the causal relationship may be one of optimal mixture, where both excess and deficiency reduce light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The commentator points out that al-Razi overlooked a possibility. In your own judgments about others or about theological matters, have you ever dismissed a view because it didn't fit a simple pattern? Ask yourself: am I too quick to refute? How can you cultivate the intellectual humility to consider nonlinear possibilities?
?
This passage shows that something can be harmed by both too little and too much. In your relationship with Allah, are you in danger of either extreme—neglect or overburdening yourself? Identify one area where you tend towards excess and one where you tend towards deficiency. What is one concrete action to move towards the middle?

The Third Objective: Does Air Become Illuminated?
Sanusi presents the third objective and addresses the denial and circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial debate about whether air can be illuminated by light and the defense of circularity of simultaneity (دور معية).

Core Idea
Circularity of simultaneity (دور معية) does not make a causal relationship impossible.

This lesson introduces the third objective of this section: whether air becomes illuminated by light. Some scholars denied this, arguing that illumination requires color, and air has no color. They further objected that if both light and color were mutually conditional, it would be circular. The author responds by introducing the concept of circularity of simultaneity (دور معية), which is permissible. Let's examine this foundational debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin with the Arabic text and translation. Notice how the third objective is presented and why it is placed here.
The passage is compact, so read it twice.
2
Identify the denial and its basis
Find the claim that air cannot be illuminated because it lacks color. Understand the reasoning: illumination requires a colored surface.
This is a key objection.
3
Understand the circularity objection and response
The opponent argues that if both light and color are conditions for each other, a logical circle results. The author counters that this is a circle of simultaneity (دور معية), which is permissible because both sides can be absent together.
This is a central concept in kalam causality.

The Third Objective: Whether air is illuminated by light. (Whether air is illuminated by light) or not? He only mentioned it here because what he mentioned in the second objective about the degrees of shadow depends on the illumination of air by light. Some denied it and made its condition, i.e., the condition of illumination by light, (color), and air has no color because it is simple, so it does not receive light due to the absence of its condition. And since one could say: It has passed that light is a condition for the existence of color according to the philosopher, so if color were also a condition for light, there would be circularity. He answered it by his saying: (So each) of light and color is a condition for the other, and the circularity is a circularity of simultaneity, so there is no impossibility in it because you have known the permissibility of simultaneous absence from both sides. And he disproves it, i.e., he disproves the statement of the denier, (that we see in the morning the horizon illuminated, and it is nothing but air illuminated by light. And it is answered that that is due to the vaporous particles mixed with it, i.e., with the air), and the discussion is about pure air (free from smoky, dusty, and vaporous particles that are receptive to light due to being colored in some way). And Imam al-Razi refuted it by saying that it would necessitate that air, the purer it is, the weaker the light occurring in it before sunrise and after sunset and in the courtyards of walls, and the more vapor and dust in it, the stronger its light, but the opposite is the case. And he also argued for the illumination of air by another aspect, namely that if air were not illuminated by light, then during the day one would necessarily see the stars that are opposite the direction of the sun, because the stars remain with their light, and the sense would not be affected on that assumption by a stronger light. His statement: (depends on the illumination of air by light; if you say: it is appropriate to present this objective before the second objective because it is a premise for it, I say: he did not present it only because attention to the second is greater. His statement: (and the circularity is a circularity of simultaneity) with this the Imam's argument is repelled that light is not a condition for the existence of color due to its entailment of circularity, as we transmitted in the second objective of the objectives of the first section. His statement: (And Imam al-Razi refuted it) He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: There is weakness in it, because it is possible that the cause is the mixing of particles to a specific limit; when it is exceeded, the light begins to decrease. Its gist is that excess harms it just as deficiency harms it. His statement: (and the sense is not affected etc.) It is said: It is possible that it is in the direction opposite the direction, it is possible, it prevents sensation of them. (The denier argued) that if air were illuminated by it, (it would be sensed), i.e., the air, (just as the wall illuminated by it is sensed), but air is not sensed at all, so it is not illuminated by light. Its answer is denial of the implication, because it is possible that color is a condition for sensing it, so illumination by light alone is not sufficient for seeing that which is illuminated by weak light. (And air is either not colored) at all, (or it has such a weak color that it is weaker than that of water and transparent stones, so that color is not sufficient for seeing air, while it is sufficient for its reception of light if its reception is made conditional on color. The sun is vapor illuminated by strong light, so the sense is affected by it, and for that reason the stars are not seen in it. In summary, the discussion is about pure air as passed, and this argument does not prove its illumination but rather the illumination of air absolutely. His statement: (sufficient for seeing that which is illuminated by weak light; if you say: the light that is in the air, if it is in such weakness that it is not seen, then the light resulting from it on the surface of the earth is more deserving of not being seen, and the consequent is false. We say: Imam answered it in al-Mulakhkhas: we commit to the consequent, because when we look at the wall that is not facing the sun, we see in it only the color and we do not see any of the quality in it when it is facing the sun. There is something in it, and it is possible to answer by denying the implication, so let it be pondered.)
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument about whether air becomes illuminated by light. It begins by presenting the third objective: whether air is illuminated by light. The author explains that he only included this because the degrees of shadow discussed in the second objective depend on air being illuminated. Some deny that air can be illuminated, arguing that illumination requires color, and air has no color because it is simple. They object that if both light and color were mutually conditional, there would be circularity. The response is that the circularity is one of simultaneity, which is not impossible because both can be absent together. The author then provides a refutation: we see the horizon illuminated at dawn, which is just air. But the opponent replies that this is due to vaporous particles. The discussion is about pure air. Imam al-Razi refutes this by pointing out that if the opponent were correct, purer air would have weaker light, but the opposite is true; also, if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day. Marginal notes explain that the objective is placed after the second because the second is more important; that the circularity argument repels the Imam's own reasoning elsewhere; that al-Razi's refutation has a weakness because the cause might be a specific degree of mixing; and that the argument about sensing air fails because color is needed for vision, and air has very weak color. Another objection argues that if air's light were too weak to see, then the light on the ground would also be invisible, but it is visible. The answer from al-Mulakhkhas is that we accept the consequent: we see only color on a wall not facing the sun, not the quality of light. The passage concludes with a note that this can be answered by denying the implication, requiring reflection.

Key Terms
تكيف الهواء بالضوء
اللون
الدور المعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of circularity in causal conditions are impossible.
Correction: Circularity of simultaneity (دور معية) is permissible because both conditions can be absent together; it does not entail an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given by those who deny that air can be illuminated by light?
A) Air is too thin to hold light.
B) Illumination requires color, and air has no color.
C) Light cannot travel through air.
D) Air is already luminous by nature.

Key Takeaway
The denial of air's illumination is countered by the concept of دور معية, which shows that mutual simultaneity does not equal impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a possibility—about Allah's creation, another person, or yourself—simply because your logic told you it was ‘impossible’? When you later discovered it was possible through a deeper understanding, how did that change your trust in Allah's unlimited ability? What is one area of your life where you need to stop limiting Allah by your own reasoning and submit to His wisdom?
?
Think of a relationship where two conditions seem to depend on each other (e.g., love and respect, trust and honesty). Have you ever given up on such a relationship because it felt like a hopeless circle? Reflect on how the concept of دور معية—mutual simultaneity that is not impossible—might encourage you to seek divine help in breaking the cycle or accepting a divinely ordained mutual dependence. Empirical Arguments and Refutations on Air's Illumination
Sanusi presents the empirical argument, the vaporous particle response, and al-Razi's rebuttals, followed by additional arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments for and against air being illuminated by light, including empirical observations and al-Razi's reasoning.

Core Idea
Empirical evidence and logical arguments support the illumination of air, despite objections about vaporous particles.

In this lesson, we continue the debate about air's illumination. The author presents an empirical observation: the horizon is illuminated at dawn, which is simply air. Opponents counter that this is due to vaporous particles. Imam al-Razi then offers two powerful arguments: the purity-light correlation and the visibility of stars during the day if air were not illuminated. Further arguments about sensing air and the visibility of weak light on the ground are also discussed. Let's examine these interconnected debates.

Guided Path
1
Follow the empirical argument and its response
Read the observation that we see the horizon illuminated at dawn, and the counter-argument that this is due to vaporous particles mixed with pure air.
Note the distinction between pure air and air with particles.
2
Analyze al-Razi's first argument about purity
Al-Razi argues that if the vaporous particle explanation were correct, purer air would have weaker light, but the opposite is true: pure air has stronger light.
This refutes the vaporous particle objection.
3
Analyze al-Razi's second argument about seeing stars
Al-Razi argues that if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day because their light would not be overpowered.
This is a logical argument based on the absence of a stronger light.
4
Examine the final arguments about sensing air and weak light
Review the arguments about whether air would be sensed if illuminated, and the objection that if air's light were too weak, ground light would also be invisible.
These further refine the discussion.
