مخصوص إذا تجاوز أخذ الضوء في النقصان وحاصله أنه أن يضره الإفراط كما يضره التفريط . قوله : ( والحس لم ينفعل إلخ) قيل : يجوز أن يكون في الجهة التي هي خلاف جهة يجوز يمنع من الإحساس بها ( احتج) المانع بأنه لو تكيف الهواء به (لأحس به ) أي بالهواء ( كما يحس بالجدار المتكيف به ) لكن الهواء لا يحس به أصلاً، فلا يكون متكيفاً بالضوء ) وجوابه منع الملازمة لجواز أن يكون اللون شرطاً في الإحساس به ) فلا يكون التكيف بالضوء وحده كافياً في رؤية المتكيف بالضوء الضعيف ( والهواء إما غير ملون ) بالكلية ( وإما له لون ضعيف جداً بحيث يكون له أضعف مما للماء والأحجار المشفة فلا يكون ذلك اللون كافياً في رؤية الهواء مع كفايته في قبوله للضوء إن جعل قبوله له مشروطاً باللون الشمس بخار يتكيف بالضوء القوي فالحس ينفعل به ولذا لم ير الكواكب فيها وبالجملة الكلام في الهواء الصرف كما مر وهذه الحجة لا تدل على استضاءته بل على استضاءة الهواء مطلقاً . قوله : (كافياً في رؤية للتكيف بالضوء الضعيف فإن قلت : الضوء الذي في الهواء إن كان في الضعف بحيث لا یری كان الضوء الحاصل منه في وجه الأرض أولى بأن لا يرى والتالي كاذب . قلنا : أجاب عنه الإمام في الملخص: بأنا نلتزم التالي لأنا إذا نظرنا إلى الجدار الذي لا تقابله الشمس كنا لا نرى فيه إلا اللون ولا نرى شيئاً من الكيفية فيه عند كونه في مقابلة الشمس وفيه ما فيه ويمكن الجواب بمنع الملازمة، فليتأمل.
The Third Objective: Whether air is illuminated by light. (Whether air is illuminated by light) or not? He only mentioned it here because what he mentioned in the second objective about the degrees of shadow depends on the illumination of air by light. Some denied it and made its condition, i.e., the condition of illumination by light, (color), and air has no color because it is simple, so it does not receive light due to the absence of its condition. And since one could say: It has passed that light is a condition for the existence of color according to the philosopher, so if color were also a condition for light, there would be circularity. He answered it by his saying: (So each) of light and color is a condition for the other, and the circularity is a circularity of simultaneity, so there is no impossibility in it because you have known the permissibility of simultaneous absence from both sides. And he disproves it, i.e., he disproves the statement of the denier, (that we see in the morning the horizon illuminated, and it is nothing but air illuminated by light. And it is answered that that is due to the vaporous particles mixed with it, i.e., with the air), and the discussion is about pure air (free from smoky, dusty, and vaporous particles that are receptive to light due to being colored in some way). And Imam al-Razi refuted it by saying that it would necessitate that air, the purer it is, the weaker the light occurring in it before sunrise and after sunset and in the courtyards of walls, and the more vapor and dust in it, the stronger its light, but the opposite is the case. And he also argued for the illumination of air by another aspect, namely that if air were not illuminated by light, then during the day one would necessarily see the stars that are opposite the direction of the sun, because the stars remain with their light, and the sense would not be affected on that assumption by a stronger light. His statement: (depends on the illumination of air by light; if you say: it is appropriate to present this objective before the second objective because it is a premise for it, I say: he did not present it only because attention to the second is greater. His statement: (and the circularity is a circularity of simultaneity) with this the Imam's argument is repelled that light is not a condition for the existence of color due to its entailment of circularity, as we transmitted in the second objective of the objectives of the first section. His statement: (And Imam al-Razi refuted it) He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: There is weakness in it, because it is possible that the cause is the mixing of particles to a specific limit; when it is exceeded, the light begins to decrease. Its gist is that excess harms it just as deficiency harms it. His statement: (and the sense is not affected etc.) It is said: It is possible that it is in the direction opposite the direction, it is possible, it prevents sensation of them. (The denier argued) that if air were illuminated by it, (it would be sensed), i.e., the air, (just as the wall illuminated by it is sensed), but air is not sensed at all, so it is not illuminated by light. Its answer is denial of the implication, because it is possible that color is a condition for sensing it, so illumination by light alone is not sufficient for seeing that which is illuminated by weak light. (And air is either not colored) at all, (or it has such a weak color that it is weaker than that of water and transparent stones, so that color is not sufficient for seeing air, while it is sufficient for its reception of light if its reception is made conditional on color. The sun is vapor illuminated by strong light, so the sense is affected by it, and for that reason the stars are not seen in it. In summary, the discussion is about pure air as passed, and this argument does not prove its illumination but rather the illumination of air absolutely. His statement: (sufficient for seeing that which is illuminated by weak light; if you say: the light that is in the air, if it is in such weakness that it is not seen, then the light resulting from it on the surface of the earth is more deserving of not being seen, and the consequent is false. We say: Imam answered it in al-Mulakhkhas: we commit to the consequent, because when we look at the wall that is not facing the sun, we see in it only the color and we do not see any of the quality in it when it is facing the sun. There is something in it, and it is possible to answer by denying the implication, so let it be pondered.)
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument about whether air becomes illuminated by light. It begins by presenting the third objective: whether air is illuminated by light. The author explains that he only included this because the degrees of shadow discussed in the second objective depend on air being illuminated. Some deny that air can be illuminated, arguing that illumination requires color, and air has no color because it is simple. They object that if both light and color were mutually conditional, there would be circularity. The response is that the circularity is one of simultaneity, which is not impossible because both can be absent together. The author then provides a refutation: we see the horizon illuminated at dawn, which is just air. But the opponent replies that this is due to vaporous particles. The discussion is about pure air. Imam al-Razi refutes this by pointing out that if the opponent were correct, purer air would have weaker light, but the opposite is true; also, if air were not illuminated, we would see stars during the day. Marginal notes explain that the objective is placed after the second because the second is more important; that the circularity argument repels the Imam's own reasoning elsewhere; that al-Razi's refutation has a weakness because the cause might be a specific degree of mixing; and that the argument about sensing air fails because color is needed for vision, and air has very weak color. Another objection argues that if air's light were too weak to see, then the light on the ground would also be invisible, but it is visible. The answer from al-Mulakhkhas is that we accept the consequent: we see only color on a wall not facing the sun, not the quality of light. The passage concludes with a note that this can be answered by denying the implication, requiring reflection.

Key Terms
الأجزاء البخارية
الهواء الصرف
الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's argument about seeing stars during the day?
A) Stars are not visible because they are too far.
B) If air were not illuminated, stars would be visible because no stronger light would overpower them.
C) Stars are only visible at night due to the absence of sunlight.
D) The stars' light is too weak to be seen through air.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's arguments and the subsequent responses demonstrate that air does become illuminated by light, directly or indirectly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the dawn light spreading across the sky, do you ever pause to consider that it is a sign of a precise Creator who established laws that your senses can verify? How will you train yourself to see such everyday phenomena as proofs of His wisdom?
?
The denier in this lesson used a plausible argument (air is not sensed, so it cannot be illuminated) that was refuted by deeper reasoning. Are there beliefs you hold about your faith or your life that, under honest scrutiny, might be based on incomplete evidence? Will you examine them this week, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
Al-Razi allowed empirical observation to correct a flawed theory. Are you willing to let evidence and sound reasoning correct your assumptions, even when it means admitting you were wrong? What is one assumption you can test today with fresh eyes?

The Proximate Cause of Sound: Waving of Air
Al-Jurjani defines sound's proximate cause and defends it against objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sound's proximate cause is the waving of air as a series of impacts and stillnesses, and you will grasp the causal argument, al-Razi's objection from probability, and the reply distinguishing the wave from visible motion.

Core Idea
Sound arises from successive impacts and stillnesses in air, not from continuous motion.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary delves into the physical cause of sound. Al-Jurjani defines the waving of air as a specific oscillatory motion—impact after impact—and argues that it is the proximate cause based on constant correlation. However, he also engages with al-Razi's objection that such correlation only yields probability, and defends his view by distinguishing the wave from visible motion. This lesson unpacks the definition, the causal argument, and the subtle epistemological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the text, then read the English translation closely. Note the technical terms.
2
Understand the definition of 'tammawuj'
Al-Jurjani defines waving as 'impact after impact and stillness after stillness'—not a simple linear motion. Compare it to ripples in water.
The term tammawuj (تموج) is key.
3
Grasp the causal argument by constant correlation
Notice that whenever waving occurs, sound occurs; when it ceases, sound ceases. Examples: air from throat, instruments, ringing basin.
This is the evidence for proximate causality.
4
Consider al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi points out that constant correlation gives only probability (zann), not certainty (yaqin). Reflect on why certainty might be required.
5
Analyze the reply on visibility
Al-Jurjani's reply has two parts: (1) motion of a visible object is visible, but the air wave is not a visible object; (2) the wave lacks sufficient color to be seen. This shows the wave is not sound itself.
The reply defends that the wave is a cause, not the sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسببه) أي سبب الصوت ( القريب تموج الهواء وليس تموجه ) هذا ( حركة ) انتقالية من هواء واحد بعينه ( بل هو صدم بعد صدم وسكون بعد سكون ) فهو حالة شبيهة الماء في الحوض إذا ألقي حجر في وسطه . وإنما جعل التموج سبباً قريباً له بتموج لأنك متى حصل التموج المذكور حصل الصوت وإذا انتقى انتفى فإنا نجد الصوت مستمراً باستمرار تموج الهواء الخارج من الحلق والآلات الصناعية ومنقطعاً بانقطاعه وكذا الحال في طنين الطست فإنه إذا سكن انقطع لانقطاع تموج الهواء حينئذ. قال الإمام الرازي: وأنت خبير بأنَّ الدوران لا يفيد إلا الظن والمسألة مما يطلب فيه اليقين. يقال : الحركة من شأنها أن تكون مبصرة ولو ثانياً فلا تكون من المعاني التي تدركها القوة الوهمية لأنا نقول : ما ذكرتم إنما هو في حركة المتحرك المحسوس بالبصر وهاهنا ليس كذلك، فلا يكون من شأن حركته أن تكون مبصرة إلى هاهنا كلامه . ثم إِنَّ المبصر نوع التموج لا تموج الهواء لعدم اللون الكافي للرؤية فيه وهذا القدر يكفي في الاستدلال على أن التموج ليس بصوت
And its proximate cause is the waving of air, and its waving is not a transitional motion of one and the same air, but rather it is impact after impact and stillness after stillness. It is a state similar to water in a basin when a stone is thrown into its middle. He only made the wave a proximate cause because whenever the mentioned waving occurs, sound occurs, and when it is absent, sound is absent. For we find sound continuing with the continuation of the waving of air exiting from the throat and industrial instruments, and ceasing with its cessation. The same is the case with the ringing of a basin, for when it becomes still it ceases due to the cessation of the waving of air then. Imam al-Razi said: You are aware that correlation yields only probability, and the matter requires certainty. It is said: motion is of the nature to be visible, even secondarily, so it is not among the meanings perceived by the estimative faculty. We say: what you mentioned applies only to the motion of a moving object perceptible by sight, but here it is not so, so its motion is not of the nature to be visible. Up to here is his speech. Then, what is visible is a type of wave, not the waving of air, due to the lack of sufficient color for vision in it. And this much suffices as evidence that the wave is not sound.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a causal explanation and defense. Al-Jurjani states that the proximate cause of sound is the waving of air, but clarifies that this waving is not a linear motion of a single air mass; rather it consists of successive impacts and subsequent stillnesses, analogous to ripples in water when a stone is dropped. He justifies the causality by constant correlation: whenever the waving occurs, sound is heard; when it stops, sound stops. Examples include the air from the throat and musical instruments, as well as the ringing of a basin. However, he notes al-Razi's objection that correlation only gives probability, not certainty. He then addresses an objection: if the wave is motion, and motion is visible (even indirectly), then the wave should be visible, yet the estimative faculty perceives it. The reply is that the visibility of motion applies only to the motion of a body that is itself visible by sight, but here the air wave lacks sufficient color to be seen, so it is not visible. Therefore, the wave is not sound. This entire discussion reinforces that sound is not the wave itself.

Key Terms
تموج
صدم
سكون
دوران
ظن
يقين
قوة وهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The waving of air is a linear motion of the same air mass.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies it is a series of impacts and stillnesses, like ripples in water, not a continuous translation.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani mean by saying the waving of air is 'impact after impact and stillness after stillness'?
A) The air moves in a straight line continuously.
B) The air vibrates with repeated collisions and pauses.
C) The air is completely still and sound occurs spontaneously.
D) The air rotates in a circle.

Key Takeaway
Sound's proximate cause is the specific oscillatory motion of air called tammawuj, defined as impact after impact, and its causal status is defended against al-Razi's probabilistic critique by arguing that the motion of air is not visible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a sound, do you ever pause to reflect that its cause is a precise sequence of impacts and stillnesses in the air? How does understanding the complexity of created things strengthen your conviction that they have a Creator?
?
Al-Razi warned that correlation gives only probability, not certainty. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah that are based on correlation instead of certain proofs? What will you do to seek certainty?
?
This lesson distinguishes between visible motion of objects and invisible motion of air. What aspects of Allah's creation remain hidden from your senses? How does that hidden reality call you to trust in revelation more than your senses?

The Cause of Sound: Air Vibration and the Certainty of Causation
The commentator establishes the cause of sound as air vibration and defends it against the objection of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator establishes air vibration as the near cause of sound and defends the certainty of this causal link against the objection of circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction plus strong intuition yields certainty about causation.

The passage begins by identifying the near cause of sound: the vibration of air, which is a series of impacts and stillnesses, not a simple motion. The commentator then presents evidence of constant conjunction to prove causation, but faces an objection from Imam al-Razi that such reasoning only yields conjecture. The response argues that the objection is incomplete and that, combined with intuitive induction, the causal link can be known with certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of sound's cause
Read the first part of the passage to find the definition of the near cause of sound: the vibration of air. Notice how it is described as 'impact after impact and stillness after stillness.'
This is not simple motion but a wave-like phenomenon.
2
Follow the proof by constant conjunction
The commentator argues that whenever vibration occurs, sound occurs; when it ceases, sound ceases. Examples are given from the throat, musical instruments, and a ringing basin.
This is the method of dawran (correlation).
3
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi objects that correlation only yields probability (zann), not certainty. He also notes that motion is usually visible, but here air motion is not.
The objection challenges the certainty of the causal link.
4
Examine the response to the objection
The response claims that the objection is incomplete: vibration can exist without sound (e.g., waving hand), and absence of vibration in some cases doesn't prove absence in all. Also, motion here is not similar to visible motion.
The response weakens the objection's force.
5
Appreciate the role of intuition (hads)
The passage concludes that induction of particular instances combined with strong intuition from penetrating minds can yield certainty, similar to many scientific matters.
This justifies the certainty of the causation for the one who intuits it, even if it is not a proof for others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وسببه) أي سبب الصوت ( القريب تموج الهواء وليس تموجه ) هذا ( حركة ) انتقالية من هواء واحد بعينه ( بل هو صدم بعد صدم وسكون بعد سكون ) فهو حالة شبيهة الماء في الحوض إذا ألقي حجر في وسطه . وإنما جعل التموج سبباً قريباً له بتموج لأنه متى حصل التموج المذكور حصل الصوت وإذا انتقى انتفى فإنا نجد الصوت مستمراً باستمرار تموج الهواء الخارج من الحلق والآلات الصناعية ومنقطعاً بانقطاعه وكذا الحال في طنين الطست فإنه إذا سكن انقطع لانقطاع تموج الهواء حينئذ. قال الإمام الرازي: وأنت خبير بأنَّ الدوران لا يفيد إلا الظن والمسألة مما يطلب فيه يقال : الحركة من شأنها أن تكون مبصرة ولو ثانياً فلا تكون من المعاني التي تدركها القوة الوهمية لأنا نقول : ما ذكرتم إنما هو في حركة المتحرك المحسوس بالبصر وهاهنا ليس كذلك، فلا يكون من شأن حركته أن تكون مبصرة إلى هاهنا كلامه . اليقين على أن الدوران هاهنا ليس بتام إما وجوداً فلأنه قد يوجد تموج الهواء باليد ولا صوت هناك وإما عدماً فلان ما ذكرتم إنما يدل على عدم الصوت في بعض صور عدم فيه التموج لا في جميعها فلا يفيد ظناً أيضاً. وقد يقال : إِنَّ استقراء بعض الجزئيات مع الحدس القوي من الأذهان الثاقبة يفيد الجزم يكون الصوت معلولاً لتموج الهواء على وجه مخصوص وكذا الحال في كثير من المسائل العلمية يستعان فيها بالحدس الفتري الصائب فلا تقوم حجة على الغير مع كونها معلومة يقيناً
And its cause, i.e., the near cause of sound, is the vibration of air, and its vibration is not a transitive motion of one and the same air, but rather it is impact after impact and stillness after stillness, so it is a state similar to water in a basin when a stone is thrown in its middle. They only made vibration a near cause for it because whenever that vibration occurs, sound occurs, and when it is absent, sound is absent; for we find sound continues with the continuation of the vibration of air coming out of the throat and artificial instruments, and ceases with its cessation. The same is the case with the ringing of a brass basin: when it becomes still, the ringing ceases due to the cessation of the vibration of air then. Imam al-Razi said: You know that circular reasoning (al-dawran) does not yield anything but conjecture, and the matter requires certainty. It is said: motion tends to be visible, even secondarily, so it would not be among the meanings perceived by the estimative faculty. We say: What you mentioned applies only to the motion of a moving object perceived by sight, but here it is not so, so its motion does not tend to be visible. This is his statement. Certainty is that the circular reasoning here is not complete—either existentially, because sometimes vibration of air exists by hand and no sound occurs, or non-existentially, because what you mentioned only indicates the absence of sound in some cases where vibration is absent, not in all, so it does not even give conjecture. And it may be said: Induction of some particulars with strong intuition from penetrating minds yields certainty that sound is an effect of air vibration in a particular manner. The same applies to many scientific matters in which sound intuition is used as an aid, so it is not a proof against others while being known with certainty.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage first states the near cause of sound: the vibration of air (تموج الهواء). The commentator clarifies that this vibration is not a simple translational motion but a series of impacts and stillnesses, like water ripples. The proof of causation is constant conjunction: whenever this vibration exists, sound exists; when it ceases, sound ceases, as seen in the throat, musical instruments, and a ringing basin. Then an objection from Imam al-Razi is raised: the reasoning based on constant conjunction (dawran) only yields probability, not certainty. The responder argues that the objection itself is flawed because motion can be visible, but here the air's motion is not visible. Furthermore, the circular reasoning is incomplete: vibration can exist without sound (e.g., waving a hand), and absence of vibration in some cases does not prove absence in all. Finally, the passage argues that strong intuition (حدس) combined with induction can yield certainty, as in many scientific matters. Thus the causal link is known with certainty to the one who intuits it, even if it cannot serve as a demonstrative proof for others.

Key Terms
سبب
تموج الهواء
دوران
حدس
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant conjunction (dawran) always yields only probability and never certainty.
Correction: The passage shows that when combined with strong intuition and induction of many particulars, one can attain certainty about a causal link, even if the raw correlation alone might only give conjecture.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objector claim that the reasoning about vibration and sound is not certain?
A) Because sound can exist without vibration
B) Because constant conjunction only yields conjecture
C) Because vibration is not physical
D) Because sound is immaterial

Key Takeaway
The commentator argues that while raw correlation may only give probability, combining induction with strong intuition can yield certainty about the causal link between air vibration and sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have probably felt certain about many things in your life—your beliefs, your opinions, your convictions. But on what basis does that certainty rest? Is it on a chain of undeniable proofs, or on a combination of repeated experience and a strong inner sense that it must be so? After reading this passage, can you identify one belief you hold that you thought was logically proven but actually relies on incomplete correlation and your own intuition? Will you examine that belief more honestly now?
?
The passage says that strong intuition from penetrating minds yields certainty, but that certainty does not constitute a proof for others. Is there an area where you are absolutely certain but cannot fully convince others? How does that make you feel? Do you become arrogant, dismissive, or defensive? This lesson calls you to humility: your certainty, though real, is not necessarily transferable. How will you hold your certainty with both confidence and humility from now on?
?
When you read the objection that dawran only gives conjecture, did you feel a moment of doubt about your own reasoning? How often do you accept the reality of a causal relationship—like 'prayer brings peace' or 'hard work leads to success'—based on repeated correlation without ever checking if it is truly the cause? Today, this lesson challenges you to examine one such relationship in your life. Will you trace it back to its supposed cause and ask if your certainty is justified, or will you continue assuming it is proven?

Violent Tearing and Striking as Causes of Vibration
Explains the two causes and mechanism of air vibration for sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how violent tearing and striking cause air vibration and sound, including the ripple-like propagation and eventual cessation.

Core Idea
Sound arises from air vibration caused by violent tearing or striking.

This passage begins by identifying the two causes of vibration that produce sound: violent tearing and violent striking. It then explains the mechanism by which these actions set the air into motion, creating a wave that propagates outward until it can no longer move the air. The analogy of a stone thrown into water illustrates this perfectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two causes and how they are defined.
Tearing is separation; striking is pressing.
2
Understand the mechanism
The passage explains that air is displaced violently to the sides, and adjacent air follows, creating a cascade of vibration.
The word 'ينفلت' means 'escapes' – air rushes sideways.
3
Examine the analogy
The stone thrown into water creates ripples that eventually die out. Similarly, sound vibrations fade when they reach air that does not yield.
This shows sound does not travel infinitely.
4
Identify the key conclusion
Both tearing and striking produce vibration, but the passage hints striking yields a more expansive wave.
This sets up the next lesson on the shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وسبب التموج المذكور قلع عنيف) أي تفريق شديد ( أو قرع عنيف) أي إمساك شديد وإنما كانا سببين للتموج (إذ بهما ينفلت الهواء من المسافة التي يسلكها الجسم) القارع أو المقروع ( إلى الجنبتين) بعنف ( وينقاد له ( أي لذلك الهواء المنفلت (ما يجاوره ) من الهواء فيقع هناك التموج المذكور، وهكذا تتصادم الأهوية وتتموج إلى أن ننتهي إلى هواء لا ينقاد للتموج، فينقطع هناك الصوت ولا يتعداه ( كالحجر المرمي في ( وسط (الماء) فظهر أنَّ كل واحد من القرع والقلع لتموج الهواء وإن كان التموج القرعي أشد انبساطاً من التموج العلقي وذكر بعضهم أن الهواء المتموج بهما على هيئة مخروط قاعدته على سطح الأرض، إذا كان المصوت ملاصقاً
And the cause of the aforementioned vibration is a violent tearing, i.e., a strong separation, or a violent striking, i.e., a strong pressing. They are both causes of vibration because by them air escapes from the distance that the body (the striker or the struck) travels to the two sides violently, and the air adjacent to it gives way to that escaping air, so there occurs that vibration. Thus the airs collide and vibrate until they reach air that does not yield to vibration, so sound ceases there and does not go beyond it, like a stone thrown into the middle of water. So it becomes apparent that each of striking and tearing produces vibration of the air, although the vibration from striking is more expansive than the vibration from tearing. And some mentioned that the air vibrating from them takes the shape of a cone whose base is on the surface of the earth, when the source of sound is adjacent.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the cause of the vibration that produces sound: either violent tearing (قلع عنيف) or violent striking (قرع عنيف). Tearing means strong separation, while striking means strong pressing. The mechanism: the moving body displaces air violently to the sides, and adjacent air follows, creating a vibration. This process continues as successive layers of air vibrate until reaching air that can no longer be set into motion, at which point sound stops. The analogy of a stone thrown into water illustrates the ripple effect. The passage then notes that the vibration from striking is more expansive than from tearing. Finally, it mentions the opinion that the vibrating air takes a conical shape with its base on the ground, provided the sound source is adjacent to the ground.

Key Terms
قلع عنيف
قرع عنيف
تموج
هواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound travels in a straight line like a beam.
Correction: This lesson shows sound propagates as a wave of vibrating air layers, spreading outward like ripples in water.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two causes of vibration?
A) Tearing and pressing
B) Pushing and pulling
C) Expansion and contraction
D) Heating and cooling

Key Takeaway
Sound is produced by air vibration caused by violent tearing or striking, and it propagates in diminishing waves like ripples in water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a walking cause of sound—every word you speak, every step you take involves violent tearing or striking of air. When you use your voice, do you reflect that each sound is a created accident subject to Allah's laws? How might this awareness change what you say and how you say it from this moment onward?
?
The passage describes sound diminishing until it meets air that does not yield. In your spiritual life, is your heart like that yielding air that allows truth to propagate, or like the resistant air that stops the sound? What specific truth from this lesson will you act on today?

The Conical Shape of Vibrating Air
Compares striking and tearing vibration and describes conical shape of vibrating air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that vibrating air from tearing or striking takes a conical shape, with the base on the ground when the sound source is adjacent.

Core Idea
Vibrating air forms a cone, not a sphere.

After establishing the causes and propagation of sound, the passage adds a refinement: the vibration from striking is more expansive than from tearing. It also records a claim that the vibrating air takes the shape of a cone whose base is on the earth's surface, when the sound source is adjacent to the ground. This points to an early understanding of sound wave geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage portion
Focus on the comparison between striking and tearing, and the description of the conical shape.
Note the word 'أشد انبساطاً' means more expansive/spread out.
2
Compare the two vibrations
Striking produces a more expansive vibration than tearing. Why? The text doesn't say, but it's an observation.
This may relate to the nature of the action.
3
Understand the conical shape claim
Some scholars say the vibrating air forms a cone with its base on the ground, provided the sound source is adjacent to the ground.
The text is incomplete, but the idea is clear.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows early attempts to model sound propagation geometrically, anticipating wavefront shapes.
Modern acoustics describes spherical waves, but here a cone is suggested due to ground reflection.

Sound as Undulation: Definition and Certainty
Defines sound as undulation and defends against circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sound as undulation and how the author defends it against circularity using induction and intuition.

Core Idea
Sound is undulation of air, but not every undulation produces sound.

This lesson introduces the first objective: defining sound. The author states that sound is undulation and immediately addresses a logical challenge. Through induction and strong intuition, he establishes that a specific kind of undulation causes sound, a certainty known to the knower though not demonstrable to others.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and objection
Read the Arabic passage where sound is defined as undulation, and note the problem of circular reasoning presented.
2
Identify the response using induction and intuition
Notice how the author uses induction of particulars with strong intuition (al-hads) to establish certainty despite the logical objection.
This intuition is described as coming from sharp minds.
3
Reflect on the epistemological status
Consider why this knowledge is certain to the knower but not a proof for others, as stated in the passage.
This highlights the role of personal certainty vs. public demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج. اليقين على أن الدوران هاهنا ليس بتام إما وجوداً فلأنه قد يوجد تموج الهواء باليد ولا صوت هناك وإما عدماً فلان ما ذكرتم إنما يدل على عدم الصوت في بعض صور عدم فيه التموج لا في جميعها فلا يفيد ظناً أيضاً. وقد يقال : إِنَّ استقراء بعض الجزئيات مع الحدس القوي من الأذهان الثاقبة يفيد الجزم يكون الصوت معلولاً لتموج الهواء على وجه مخصوص وكذا الحال في كثير من المسائل العلمية يستعان فيها بالحدس الفتري الصائب فلا تقوم حجة على الغير مع كونها معلومة يقيناً. وسبب التموج المذكور قلع عنيف أي تفريق شديد أو قرع عنيف أي إمساك شديد. إذ بهما ينفلت الهواء من المسافة التي يسلكها الجسم إلى الجنبتين بعنف وينقاد له ما يجاوره من الهواء فيقع هناك التموج المذكور. وهكذا تتصادم الأهوية وتتموج إلى أن ننتهي إلى هواء لا ينقاد للتموج، فينقطع هناك الصوت ولا يتعداه. فظهر أن كل واحد من القرع والقلع لتموج الهواء وإن كان التموج القرعي أشد انبساطاً من التموج العلقي. وذكر بعضهم أن الهواء المتموج بهما على هيئة مخروط قاعدته على سطح الأرض، إذا كان المصوت ملاصقاً. قوله: قاعدته على سطح الأرض الخ، فإن قلت: ما الدليل على أن الهواء المتموج بهما على هيئة المخروط وليس على هيئة أسطوانة مستديرة أحد جانبيها على الأرض والآخر في جانب السماء؟ قلت: الدليل عليه أنك إذا صوتت في موضع من الأرض وفرضنا أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه صوتك من كل جانب نصف فرسخ فالهواء المتموج من جوانبك على هيئة دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها في موضعك ولا شك أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه الصوت من جهة العلو مما يحاذي رأسك نصف فرسخ أيضاً فلو كان الهواء المتموج كأسطوانة مستديرة يكون أيضاً جانبها الذي يلي السماء دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها ما يحاذي تلك الدائرة وليس كذلك لأن البعد بينك وبين محيطة تلك الدائرة أزيد من نصف فرسخ فتعين أن الهواء المتموج على هيئة مخروطة كما ذكره. قيل: وإنما لم يجعلوهما سببين للصوت ابتداء حتى يكون التموج والوصول إلى السامعة سبباً للإحساس به لا لوجوده في نفسه بناء على أن القرع وصول والقلع لا وصول وهما آنيان، فلا يجوز كونهما سببين للصوت لأنه زماني. ورد ذلك بأن التموج إن كان آنياً فقد جعلوه سبباً للصوت الزماني وإن كان زمانياً فقد جعلوا القرع والقلع الآنيين سبباً له فجعل الآني سبباً للزماني لازم على كل تقدير ولا محذور فيه إذا لم يكن السبب علة تامة أو جزءاً أخيراً منها إذ لا يلزم حينئذ أن يكون الزمان موجوداً في الآن.
The third type - the first section: the first objective: Sound is undulation (al-tamawwuj). Certainty that the circulation here is not complete; either in existence because undulation of air may exist by hand and yet no sound there, or in absence because what you mentioned only indicates the absence of sound in some pictures where undulation is absent, not in all of them, so it does not even yield conjecture. And it may be said: Indeed, induction of some particulars with strong intuition (al-hads) from sharp minds yields certainty that sound is caused by undulation of air in a particular manner, and likewise the case in many scientific matters in which sound intuition is employed, so it does not constitute a proof against others while being known with certainty. And the cause of the mentioned undulation is violent separation (qal' 'anif) i.e. intense dispersion, or violent striking (qar' 'anif) i.e. intense holding. For by them the air escapes from the distance that the body traverses to the two sides violently, and what is adjacent to it of the air yields to it, so there occurs the mentioned undulation. And thus the airs collide and undulate until they reach air that does not yield to undulation, so sound ceases there and does not go beyond. So it became apparent that each of striking and separation causes undulation of air, although the undulation from striking is more expansive than the undulation from separation. And some mentioned that the air undulating by them is in the form of a cone whose base is on the ground, if the sound source is adjacent. His saying: 'its base on the ground etc.' Then if you say: What is the proof that the air undulating by them is in the form of a cone and not in the form of a circular cylinder with one side on the ground and the other in the direction of the sky? I say: The proof for that is that if you produce sound in a place on the ground and we assume that the limit of what your sound reaches from every side is half a parasang, then the undulating air from your sides is in the form of a circle with a diameter of one parasang and its center at your location, and there is no doubt that the limit of what the sound reaches from the direction of the height opposite your head is also half a parasang. So if the undulating air were like a circular cylinder, its side facing the sky would also be a circle with a diameter of one parasang and its center opposite that circle, but it is not so, because the distance between you and the circumference of that circle is greater than half a parasang. So it is determined that the undulating air is in the form of a cone as he mentioned. It was said: Why did they not make them causes of sound initially, so that the undulation and arrival at the hearing organ would be a cause for sensing it, not for its existence in itself, based on the fact that striking involves contact and separation does not, and they are instantaneous (āniyān), so it is not permissible for them to be causes of sound because it is temporal (zamānī). And that was refuted by saying: If undulation is instantaneous, they have made it a cause of temporal sound, and if it is temporal, they have made the instantaneous striking and separation a cause for it, so making the instantaneous a cause of the temporal is necessary in every case, and there is no harm in it if the cause is not a complete cause or a final part of it, for then it is not necessary that time exist in the instant.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning sound. It begins by stating the first objective: sound is undulation (al-tamawwuj). The author then addresses a potential objection about circular reasoning: the claim that if sound is undulation, then the absence of undulation implies absence of sound, but the converse is not always true because undulation can exist without sound (e.g., by hand movement). The response uses induction and strong intuition (al-hads al-qawī) to establish a particular kind of undulation as the cause of sound, which is known with certainty to the knower even if not provable to others. Next, the cause of this undulation is given: violent separation (qal' 'anif) or violent striking (qar' 'anif). The mechanism is explained: these actions cause the air to escape violently to the sides, and adjacent air yields, creating a wave of undulation that propagates until it meets air that does not yield, where sound stops. It is noted that undulation from striking is more expansive. A geometrical model is then presented: the undulating air forms a cone with its base on the ground if the sound source is on the ground. A proof is given: if sound reaches a certain distance horizontally and vertically, the shape cannot be a cylinder but must be a cone because the distance from the source to the edge of the horizontal circle is the radius of that circle, while the distance to the edge of the vertical circle would be a hypotenuse longer than the radius. Finally, an objection is raised: why not consider striking and separation as causes of the sensation of sound rather than its existence, since they are instantaneous while sound is temporal? The refutation states that whether undulation is instantaneous or temporal, in either case an instantaneous cause can produce a temporal effect if it is not a complete cause or the final part of it; there is no requirement that time exist in the instant.

Key Terms
صوت
تموج
حدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any undulation of air produces sound.
Correction: The passage specifies that only undulation caused by a specific cause (to be explained later) produces sound, and that undulation can exist without sound (e.g., by hand).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what method is used to gain certainty that sound is caused by undulation?
A) Strict logical deduction
B) Induction of particulars with strong intuition
C) Empirical experimentation alone
D) Divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Sound is defined as undulation of air, and certainty about this definition comes from induction and intuition, not from a purely deductive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you relied on sound intuition to be certain of something about your faith, even though you couldn't convince others? Does that make you doubt, or does it strengthen your certainty?
?
Consider your belief in the unseen aspects of Aqeedah. Are you comfortable that some certainties come from intuition rather than rigorous proofs? How does that affect your conviction?
?

The Cause of Sound: Violent Separation or Striking
Explains the cause of undulation as violent separation or striking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cause of sound as violent separation or striking, and how these actions produce undulation in air.

Core Idea
Sound arises from violent separation or striking that forces air to undulate.

This lesson focuses on the cause of the undulation that constitutes sound. The author identifies two physical actions: violent separation (qal' 'anif) and violent striking (qar' 'anif). He explains how these cause the air to escape violently to the sides and propagate as a wave until it loses energy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes of undulation
Read the passage and note that the cause of undulation is either violent separation or violent striking. Understand the Arabic terms: قلع عنيف and قرع عنيف.
Separation refers to pulling apart, striking refers to impact.
2
Understand the mechanism of wave propagation
The explanation describes how air escapes to the sides and adjacent air yields, creating a cascade of undulations until the air no longer yields, at which point sound stops.
This is an early model of sound wave propagation.
3
Note the difference in expansiveness
The passage states that undulation from striking is more expansive than from separation. Consider why this might be.

في موضع من الأرض وفرضنا أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه صوتك من كل جانب نصف فرسخ فالهواء المتموج من جوانبك على هيئة دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها في موضعك ولا شك أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه الصوت من جهة العلو مما يحاذي رأسك نصف فرسخ أيضاً فلو كان الهواء المتموج كأسطوانة مستديرة يكون أيضاً جانبها الذي يلي السماء دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها ما يحاذي تلك الدائرة وليس كذلك لأن البعد بينك وبين محيطة تلك الدائرة أزيد من نصف فرسخ فتعين أن الهواء المتموج على هيئة مخروطة كما ذكره. قيل: وإنما لم يجعلوهما سببين للصوت ابتداء حتى يكون التموج والوصول إلى السامعة سبباً للإحساس به لا لوجوده في نفسه بناء على أن القرع وصول والقلع لا وصول وهما آنيان، فلا يجوز كونهما سببين للصوت لأنه زماني. ورد ذلك بأن التموج إن كان آنياً فقد جعلوه سبباً للصوت الزماني وإن كان زمانياً فقد جعلوا القرع والقلع الآنيين سبباً له فجعل الآني سبباً للزماني The third type - the first section: the first objective: Sound is undulation (al-tamawwuj). Certainty that the circulation here is not complete; either in existence because undulation of air may exist by hand and yet no sound there, or in absence because what you mentioned only indicates the absence of sound in some pictures where undulation is absent, not in all of them, so it does not even yield conjecture. And it may be said: Indeed, induction of some particulars with strong intuition (al-hads) from sharp minds yields certainty that sound is caused by undulation of air in a particular manner, and likewise the case in many scientific matters in which sound intuition is employed, so it does not constitute a proof against others while being known with certainty. And the cause of the mentioned undulation is violent separation (qal' 'anif) i.e. intense dispersion, or violent striking (qar' 'anif) i.e. intense holding. For by them the air escapes from the distance that the body traverses to the two sides violently, and what is adjacent to it of the air yields to it, so there occurs the mentioned undulation. And thus the airs collide and undulate until they reach air that does not yield to undulation, so sound ceases there and does not go beyond. So it became apparent that each of striking and separation causes undulation of air, although the undulation from striking is more expansive than the undulation from separation. And some mentioned that the air undulating by them is in the form of a cone whose base is on the ground, if the sound source is adjacent. His saying: 'its base on the ground etc.' Then if you say: What is the proof that the air undulating by them is in the form of a cone and not in the form of a circular cylinder with one side on the ground and the other in the direction of the sky? I say: The proof for that is that if you produce sound in a place on the ground and we assume that the limit of what your sound reaches from every side is half a parasang, then the undulating air from your sides is in the form of a circle with a diameter of one parasang and its center at your location, and there is no doubt that the limit of what the sound reaches from the direction of the height opposite your head is also half a parasang. So if the undulating air were like a circular cylinder, its side facing the sky would also be a circle with a diameter of one parasang and its center opposite that circle, but it is not so, because the distance between you and the circumference of that circle is greater than half a parasang. So it is determined that the undulating air is in the form of a cone as he mentioned. It was said: Why did they not make them causes of sound initially, so that the undulation and arrival at the hearing organ would be a cause for sensing it, not for its existence in itself, based on the fact that striking involves contact and separation does not, and they are instantaneous (āniyān), so it is not permissible for them to be causes of sound because it is temporal (zamānī). And that was refuted by saying: If undulation is instantaneous, they have made it a cause of temporal sound, and if it is temporal, they have made the instantaneous striking and separation a cause for it, so making the instantaneous a cause of the temporal is necessary in every case, and there is no harm in it if the cause is not a complete cause or a final part of it, for then it is not necessary that time exist in the instant.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning sound. It begins by stating the first objective: sound is undulation (al-tamawwuj). The author then addresses a potential objection about circular reasoning: the claim that if sound is undulation, then the absence of undulation implies absence of sound, but the converse is not always true because undulation can exist without sound (e.g., by hand movement). The response uses induction and strong intuition (al-hads al-qawī) to establish a particular kind of undulation as the cause of sound, which is known with certainty to the knower even if not provable to others. Next, the cause of this undulation is given: violent separation (qal' 'anif) or violent striking (qar' 'anif). The mechanism is explained: these actions cause the air to escape violently to the sides, and adjacent air yields, creating a wave of undulation that propagates until it meets air that does not yield, where sound stops. It is noted that undulation from striking is more expansive. A geometrical model is then presented: the undulating air forms a cone with its base on the ground if the sound source is on the ground. A proof is given: if sound reaches a certain distance horizontally and vertically, the shape cannot be a cylinder but must be a cone because the distance from the source to the edge of the horizontal circle is the radius of that circle, while the distance to the edge of the vertical circle would be a hypotenuse longer than the radius. Finally, an objection is raised: why not consider striking and separation as causes of the sensation of sound rather than its existence, since they are instantaneous while sound is temporal? The refutation states that whether undulation is instantaneous or temporal, in either case an instantaneous cause can produce a temporal effect if it is not a complete cause or the final part of it; there is no requirement that time exist in the instant.

تموج

Checkpoint
What are the two causes of undulation mentioned in the passage?
A) Violent separation and gentle compression
B) Violent striking and gentle separation
C) Violent separation and violent striking
D) Violent tearing and gentle pushing

Key Takeaway
Sound is caused by violent separation or striking, which force air to undulate and propagate until the air can no longer yield.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every sound you hear—whether a kind word or a harsh strike—is a created undulation that only continues as long as air yields. Does this brevity remind you that your words and actions, like sound waves, have a limit and you will be accountable for them? What change can you make today in how you speak or respond to harsh sounds?
?
The undulation of air is a sign of Allah's precise creation, yet you hear hundreds of sounds daily without a second thought. Pause now: can you recall a sound that brought you closer to Allah? If not, how can you train your ears to listen for His signs in the sounds of nature, prayer, or even silence?
?
Sound ceases when air no longer yields. Is there a 'sound' in your life—a bad habit, a hurtful conversation, a distraction—that should cease because it no longer yields to goodness? What step will you take to cut it off before it propagates further?

The Geometric Shape of Sound Propagation
Proves that undulating air forms a cone, not a cylinder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that sound propagates as a cone rather than a cylinder, based on a geometric proof.

Core Idea
Sound from a point source on the ground propagates as a cone, not a cylinder.

This lesson presents a geometric model for how sound travels through air. The author argues that when a sound source is on the ground, the undulating air forms a cone with its base on the ground. He provides a proof by comparing distances, showing that a cylinder would require an impossible longer distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about cone shape
Read that some scholars mentioned the undulating air is in the form of a cone with its base on the ground, when the source is adjacent.
2
Understand the hypothetical objection
The objection suggests the shape could be a cylinder extending upward. Recognize the difference between cone and cylinder.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction
The proof assumes equal horizontal and vertical reach (half a parasang) and shows that a cylinder would yield a longer distance from the source to the top edge, contradicting the assumption.
This uses simple geometry: the distance to the top edge of a cylinder would be the hypotenuse of a right triangle, longer than the radius.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج. اليقين على أن الدوران هاهنا ليس بتام إما وجوداً فلأنه قد يوجد تموج الهواء باليد ولا صوت هناك وإما عدماً فلان ما ذكرتم إنما يدل على عدم الصوت في بعض صور عدم فيه التموج لا في جميعها فلا يفيد ظناً أيضاً. وقد يقال : إِنَّ استقراء بعض الجزئيات مع الحدس القوي من الأذهان الثاقبة يفيد الجزم يكون الصوت معلولاً لتموج الهواء على وجه مخصوص وكذا الحال في كثير من المسائل العلمية يستعان فيها بالحدس الفتري الصائب فلا تقوم حجة على الغير مع كونها معلومة يقيناً. وسبب التموج المذكور قلع عنيف أي تفريق شديد أو قرع عنيف أي إمساك شديد. إذ بهما ينفلت الهواء من المسافة التي يسلكها الجسم إلى الجنبتين بعنف وينقاد له ما يجاوره من الهواء فيقع هناك التموج المذكور. وهكذا تتصادم الأهوية وتتموج إلى أن ننتهي إلى هواء لا ينقاد للتموج، فينقطع هناك الصوت ولا يتعداه. فظهر أن كل واحد من القرع والقلع لتموج الهواء وإن كان التموج القرعي أشد انبساطاً من التموج العلقي. وذكر بعضهم أن الهواء المتموج بهما على هيئة مخروط قاعدته على سطح الأرض، إذا كان المصوت ملاصقاً. قوله: قاعدته على سطح الأرض الخ، فإن قلت: ما الدليل على أن الهواء المتموج بهما على هيئة المخروط وليس على هيئة أسطوانة مستديرة أحد جانبيها على الأرض والآخر في جانب السماء؟ قلت: الدليل عليه أنك إذا صوتت في موضع من الأرض وفرضنا أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه صوتك من كل جانب نصف فرسخ فالهواء المتموج من جوانبك على هيئة دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها في موضعك ولا شك أن منتهى ما يبلغ إليه الصوت من جهة العلو مما يحاذي رأسك نصف فرسخ أيضاً فلو كان الهواء المتموج كأسطوانة مستديرة يكون أيضاً جانبها الذي يلي السماء دائرة قطرها فرسخ مركزها ما يحاذي تلك الدائرة وليس كذلك لأن البعد بينك وبين محيطة تلك الدائرة أزيد من نصف فرسخ فتعين أن الهواء المتموج على هيئة مخروطة كما ذكره. قيل: وإنما لم يجعلوهما سببين للصوت ابتداء حتى يكون التموج والوصول إلى السامعة سبباً للإحساس به لا لوجوده في نفسه بناء على أن القرع وصول والقلع لا وصول وهما آنيان، فلا يجوز كونهما سببين للصوت لأنه زماني. ورد ذلك بأن التموج إن كان آنياً فقد جعلوه سبباً للصوت الزماني وإن كان زمانياً فقد جعلوا القرع والقلع الآنيين سبباً له فجعل الآني سبباً للزماني لازم على كل تقدير ولا محذور فيه إذا لم يكن السبب علة تامة أو جزءاً أخيراً منها إذ لا يلزم حينئذ أن يكون الزمان موجوداً في الآن.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Instantaneous Cause and Temporal Effect: A Defense
Defends instantaneous causes producing temporal sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that instantaneous causes cannot produce temporal sound, and the refutation that instantaneous causes can indeed produce temporal effects if they are not complete causes.

Core Idea
Instantaneous causes can produce temporal effects if they are not the complete cause.

This lesson addresses a philosophical objection: striking and separation are instantaneous, but sound is temporal (lasting over time). How can an instantaneous cause produce a temporal effect? The author refutes this by examining the nature of causation, arguing that an instantaneous cause can lead to a temporal effect if it is not the complete cause or the final part.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that striking and separation are instantaneous (āniyān) and involve contact or lack thereof, so they cannot cause temporal sound.
The objection proposes that they cause the sensation of sound, not its existence.
2
Understand the refutation's logic
The refutation points out that whether undulation is instantaneous or temporal, an instantaneous cause can still produce a temporal effect. The key is that the cause is not a complete cause (ʿilla tāmma) or the final part of it.
This avoids the requirement that time exist in the instant.
3
Reflect on the philosophical implications
Consider how this argument fits within the broader discussion of causality in kalam and natural philosophy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An instantaneous cause cannot produce a temporal effect at all.
Correction: The passage explains that an instantaneous cause can produce a temporal effect if it is not the complete cause or the final part of the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, under what condition can an instantaneous cause produce a temporal effect?
A) If the cause is the complete cause
B) If the cause is the final part of the complete cause
C) If the cause is not a complete cause or its final part
D) It can never produce a temporal effect

Key Takeaway
Instantaneous causes like striking and separation can produce temporal sound as long as they are not the complete cause or its final part.

?
Think of a time you considered a single action of yours as the sole reason for an outcome you achieved. How does this lesson's reminder that your action was not the complete cause change how you view that achievement? Will you now make a habit of acknowledging other factors, including divine will?
?
What 'instantaneous' decision you made in the past has had a lasting, temporal impact on your life? Are you taking full responsibility for that decision, or do you assume its effects are limited because it happened quickly? How can this lesson help you see the ripple effects of your choices?
?
When you sin, do you think 'it was just a moment, so its consequences are small'? How does the refutation of the objection challenge that thought? What does it mean for you to repent sincerely, knowing that an instantaneous sin can have lasting effects, but also that your repentance is not the complete cause of forgiveness?

Objection and Answer: Instantaneous Causes for Temporal Sound
The author refutes the objection that instantaneous qar' and qal' cannot cause temporal sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why instantaneous causes can produce temporal effects in kalam causality theory, and how the arguments and rebuttals reinforce that position.

Core Idea
Instantaneous causes can produce temporal effects if they are not complete causes or final parts.

This passage addresses a key objection in kalam physics: how can instantaneous events like contact (qarʿ) and separation (qalʿ) cause a temporal phenomenon like sound? The objection seems intuitive, but the author systematically shows that the objection fails and the position is consistent. We'll follow the dialectical back-and-forth to grasp the underlying causal principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial rebuttal
Read the first part: the objection that qar' and qal' cannot cause temporal sound, and the initial rebuttal that an instantaneous cause for a temporal effect is unproblematic if not complete/final.
Note the key terms: ʿilla tāmma (complete cause) and juzʾ akhīr (final part).
2
Follow the sub-objection about sound's temporality
Next, a sub-objection from the author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif claims some sounds are instantaneous. See how it is dismissed by distinguishing letters from sound.
This shows the importance of precise definitions.
3
Analyze the third objection and its answer
Then a deeper objection about the undulation (tamawwuj) being intermediate, and the answer that the problem only arises if the instantaneous cause is complete or final.
The answer relies on denying that qar' and qal' are complete/final causes.
4
Synthesize the overall argument
Combine the pieces to see how the author defends the original position that instantaneous causes can produce temporal effects under certain conditions.
This reinforces the nuanced kalam view of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : وإنما لم يجعلوهما سببين للصوت ابتداء حتى يكون التموج والوصول إلى السامعة سبباً للإحساس به لا لوجوده في نفسه بناء على أنَّ القرع وصول والقلع لا وصول وهما آنيان، فلا يجوز كونهما سببين للصوت لأنه زماني، ورد ذلك بأن التموج إن كان آنياً فقد جعلوه سبباً للصوت الزماني وإن كان زمانياً فقد جعلوا القرع والقلع الآنيين سبباً له فجعل الآني سبباً للزماني لازم على كل تقدير ولا محذور فيه إذا لم يكن السبب علة تامة أو جزءاً أخيراً منها إذ لا يلزم حينئذ أن يكون الزمان موجوداً في الآن . قوله : ( فلا يجوز كونهما سبباً للصوت لأنه زماني ) قال صاحب الصحائف : فيه بحث إذ لا نسلم أن الصوت زماني لأنَّ بعض الحروف آني كما يجيء مع أنه صوت ولا يخفى عليك اندفاعه بما مر من أنَّ الحرف عارض للصوت لا نفسه. قوله : ( أو جزءاً أخيراً منها ) قيل : لا شك أن كلاً من الوصول واللاوصول جزء أخير لعلة التموج فإذا كانا آنيين يلزم أن يكون الجزء الأخير آنياً والمعلول زمانياً ولو سلم أنه ليس بجزء أخير فمجرد الجزئية مع كونه آنياً يستلزم المحذور لأن المتوسط بين ذلك الجزء الآتي والمعلول الزماني أعني التموج، إما أن يكون آنياً أو زمانياً فالمحذور ثابت والجواب عن الأول المنع وعن الثاني بأن المحذور على تقدير توسط الزماني إنما يلزم إذا جعل ذلك الآني علة تامة للمتوسط الزماني أو جزءاً أخيراً منها وهو ممنوع
It was said: Why did they not make them (qar' and qal') causes for sound ab initio, so that the undulation and arrival at the hearing sense would be the cause of sensation of it, not of its existence in itself, based on the fact that qar' involves contact and qal' involves separation, and both are instantaneous, so it is not permissible for them to be causes of sound because sound is temporal. This was rebutted by saying that if the undulation is instantaneous, then they have made it a cause of temporal sound, and if it is temporal, then they have made the instantaneous qar' and qal' its cause, so an instantaneous cause for a temporal effect is necessary in any case, and there is no problem in that if the cause is not a complete cause (ʿilla tāmma) or a final part (juzʾ akhīr) of it, for then it would not entail that time exists in the instant. His statement: 'So it is not permissible for them to be causes of sound because sound is temporal' – the author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif said: There is a discussion here, for we do not concede that sound is temporal, because some letters are instantaneous, as will come, yet they are sound. And it is not hidden from you that this is repelled by what has passed, that the letter is an accident of sound, not sound itself. His statement: 'or a final part of it' – it was said: There is no doubt that each of contact and separation is a final part of the cause of undulation; so if they are instantaneous, it follows that the final part is instantaneous and the effect is temporal; and even if it is conceded that it is not a final part, mere partiality with it being instantaneous entails the problem, because the intermediate between that instantaneous part and the temporal effect, namely the undulation, is either instantaneous or temporal, so the problem remains. The answer to the first is a denial, and to the second: that the problem, given the intermediation of the temporal, only follows if that instantaneous thing is made a complete cause of the temporal intermediate or a final part of it – and that is denied.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and answers concerning the causality of instantaneous events (qarʿ and qalʿ) in producing temporal sound. The initial objection states that since qar' (contact) and qal' (separation) are instantaneous (āniyān), they cannot be causes of temporal sound. The rebuttal responds that the undulation (tamawwuj) of air is either instantaneous or temporal; in either case an instantaneous cause for a temporal effect is entailed, and it is unproblematic as long as the cause is not a complete cause (ʿilla tāmma) or a final part (juzʾ akhīr). A subsidiary objection from the author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif claims that some sounds (letters) are instantaneous, but the answer clarifies that letters are accidents of sound, not sound itself. A third objection argues that even if the contact/separation are final parts, the intermediate undulation could be instantaneous or temporal, leading to a problem. The answer denies that they are final parts and maintains that the problem only arises if the instantaneous thing is a complete cause or final part of the temporal intermediate, which is denied. Thus the original position that instantaneous causes can produce temporal effects stands.

زماني
علة تامة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any instantaneous cause cannot produce a temporal effect.
Correction: The passage shows it is possible if the cause is not a complete cause or final part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can an instantaneous cause produce a temporal effect?
A) Never, cause and effect must share the same temporality.
B) Only if the cause is a complete cause.
C) If the cause is not a complete cause or a final part of it.
D) Only if the effect is also instantaneous.

Key Takeaway
Instantaneous causes can yield temporal effects provided they are not complete causes or final parts of the causal chain.

Three Proofs That Sound Perception Requires Carrier Air
Sanusi gives three proofs that sound requires carrier air, with objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs from kalam that sound perception depends on the carrier air reaching the ear, along with the objections and replies that refine the argument.

Core Idea
Sound perception necessarily requires carrier air reaching the ear.

Sanusi presents a foundational argument in the physics of perception: sound is not directly perceived but requires a medium. He first clarifies the mechanism—a chain of air undulations—then offers three empirical proofs. However, the arguments are not without objections, and the passage engages with critical responses from earlier scholars, sharpening the causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mechanism
Read the opening explanation of how sound is transmitted through a chain of undulating air, not a single wave.
This sets the conceptual foundation for the proofs.
2
Examine the first proof (tube experiment)
Review the tube experiment and the objection that the tube might block sensory attachment rather than air flow.
Notice how the reply reaffirms the role of confined air.
3
Examine the second proof (wind inclination)
Consider the example of the muezzin's voice being carried by wind, and the objection that we hear even when wind diverts air away.
The reply uses the concept of disturbed hearing to prove air still reaches.
4
Examine the third proof (temporal delay)
Study the delay between striking an axe and hearing its sound, and al-Razi's objection that these proofs only show correlation.
Reflect on the methodological issue of correlation vs. causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود أن الإحساس بالصوت يتوقف على أن يصل الهواء الحامل له إلى الصماخ لا بمعنى أن هواء واحداً بعينه يتموج ويتكيف بالصوت ويوصله إلى القوة السامعة بل أن ما يجاور ذلك الهواء المتكيف بالصوت يتموج ويتكيف بالصوت أيضاً. وهكذا إلى أن يتموج ويتكيف به الهواء الراكد في الصماخ فتدركه السامعة حينئذ وإنما قلنا : إن الاحساس بالصوت يتوقف على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى حاسة السامع ( لوجوه الأول : أن من وضع فمه في طرف أنبوبة طويلة (و) وضع طرفها الآخر في صماخ إنسان وتكلم فيه بصوت عال (سمعه) ذلك الإنسان ( دون غيره ) من الحاضرين وإن كانوا أقرب إلى المتكلم من ذلك الإنسان وما هو إلا لحصرها أي ليس سماعه للصوت دون غيره إلا لحصر الأنبوبة الهواء الحامل للصوت ومنعها إياه من الانتشار والوصول إلى صماخ الغير فلا يصل إلا إلى صماخ ذلك الإنسان فلا يسمعه إلا هو . ( الثاني : أنه ( أعني الصوت ) يميل مع الريح كما هو المجرب في صوت المؤذن على المنارة فمن كان منه في جهة تهب الريح إليها يسمع صوته وإن كان بعيداً ومن كان في غير تلك الجهة لا يسمعه وإن تساويا في مسافة البعد وما ذلك إلا لأن الريح تميل الهواء الحامل له وتحركه إلى الجانب الذي هبت إليه فدل على أن ما ذكر من ... قوله : ( لوصوله إلى السامعة ذكره تعييناً لما عطف عليه قوله : لا لتعلق حاسة السمع. قوله : ( يتوقف على أنه يصل الهواء الحامل له إلى الصماخ اعترض عليه صاحب الصحائب بأنا ندرك أن صوت المؤذن عند هبوب الرياح يميل عن جهتنا إلى خلافها، وذلك ضروري يعرفه كل أحد بالتجربة ومن المعلوم ضرورة أن الهواء الحامل لذلك الصوت ما وصل إلى صماخنا إذ نحن وقتئذ في موضع لا ريح فيه حتى يقال : إنه صرفه عن جهتنا بل كان خارجاً عن ذلك الموضع صرفه الريح عن جهتنا فقد سمعنا صوتا مع عدم وصول الهواء الحامل لذلك الصوت إلى صماخنا، وفيه نظر لأن تشوش سماع الصوت حينئذ يدل على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى صماخنا إذ لو لم يكن الإحساس متوقفاً على ذلك الوصول لما تشوش ضرورة والتالي باطل بالتجربة فكذا المقدم. قوله : ( وما هو إلا لحصرها الخ قد يقال : لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك بمنع الأنبوبة أن يتعلق حاسة السمع بالصوت الذي في داخلها كما يمنع حاسة البصر مع رؤية ما في داخلها إذا كان فيه شيء مرئي فلا يفيد توقف الإحساس بالصوت على وصول الهواء الحامل إلى الصماخ على أنا لا نسلم عدم وصول الهواء إلى صماخ الحاضرين ولو قيل : لو وصل لسمع يمنع لجواز توقفه على شرط آخر. قوله : ( وإن تساويا في مسافة البعد إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض صاحب الصحائف بجواز أن يكون عدم السماع لبعد الصوت عن حد السماع حينئذ لأن الإدراك من البعيد لا بد أن يكون له حد كما في الإبصار فإذا جاوز المدرك ذلك الحد لا يدرك . (الثالث: أنه ) أي سماع الصوت ( يتأخر عن سببه ) أعني سبب الصوت ) تأخراً زمانياً فإنا نشاهد ضرب الفأس على الخشب من بعيد ونسمع صوته الذي يوجد معه بلا تخلف بعد ذلك بزمان يتفاوت ذلك الزمان بالقرب والبعد وما هو إلا لسلوك الهواء الحامل له في تلك المسافة حتى يصل إلى صماخنا واعترض عليه الإمام الرازي: بأن الوجوه الثلاثة راجعة إلى الدوران إذ محصولها أنه متى وجد وصول الهواء الحامل وجد السماع ومتى لم يوجد لم يوجد، فلا يفيد إلا ظناً. وقد سبق أن مثلها يحتاج إلى حدس ليفيد جزماً. قوله : ( ونسمع صوته الذي يوجد معه بلا تخلف فيه بحث لأن وجود الصوت إذا كان مع الضرب الذي هو القرع الآني يلزم أن يكون الآني علة تامة للزماني أو جزءاً منها مستلزماً له فبه يعود الإشكال السابق. اللهم إلا أن يريد بالمعية أعم مما هو في حكمها بسبب قلة الزمان المتخلل وكذا من عدم التخلف . قوله : (وما هو إلا لسلوك الخ اعترض عليه صاحب الصحائف بجواز أن يكون عدم السماع وقت الضرب لبعد الصوت وقتئذ عند حد السماع . فإذا وصل حده سمع. نعم لو ثبت أن السماع قد يتأخر عن مشاهدة ضرب الفأس سواء كان على حد السماع أم لا، اندفع لكن إثباته عسير، ثم إنه يرد أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك لبطء تعلق حاسة السمع وسرعة تعلق حاسة البصر بسبب آخر دون توسط سلوك الهواء ؟ فتأمل .
And what is meant is that the sensation of sound depends on the carrier air reaching the ear canal, not in the sense that a single specific air wave undulates and becomes qualified with sound and conveys it to the auditory power, but rather that what is adjacent to that air qualified with sound also undulates and becomes qualified with sound, and so on until the stagnant air in the ear canal undulates and becomes qualified with it, and then the auditory power perceives it. And we only said that the sensation of sound depends on the carrier air reaching the sense of hearing for several reasons: First: that one who places his mouth at the end of a long tube and places its other end in the ear canal of a person and speaks in it with a loud voice, that person hears it without others present, even if they are closer to the speaker than that person, and that is only because the tube confines the carrier air of the sound and prevents it from spreading and reaching the ear canal of others, so it reaches only the ear canal of that person, so only he hears it. Second: that sound inclines with the wind, as is experienced in the sound of the muezzin on the minaret. So whoever is from him in a direction to which the wind blows hears his voice even if he is far, and whoever is in another direction does not hear it even if they are equal in distance, and that is only because the wind inclines the carrier air and moves it to the side to which it blew, so it indicates that what was mentioned... His saying: 'to its reaching the auditory power' he mentioned it as a specification for what is coordinated with his saying 'not due to the attachment of the sense of hearing'. His saying: 'depends on that the carrier air reaches the ear canal' - the author of al-Sahā'if objected: we perceive that the sound of the muezzin when the winds blow inclines from our direction to the opposite, and that is necessarily known by everyone through experience, and it is necessarily known that the carrier air of that sound did not reach our ear canal, since we are at that time in a place with no wind, so it is said that it diverted it from our direction, but rather it was outside that place, the wind diverted it from our direction, so we heard a sound without the carrier air of that sound reaching our ear canal. But there is consideration because the disturbance of hearing the sound at that time indicates the carrier air's reaching our ear canal, for if the sensation were not dependent on that reaching, it would not have been disturbed necessarily, and the consequent is false by experience, so the antecedent is likewise. His saying: 'and it is only due to its confinement, etc.' - it may be said: it is not permissible that this is due to the tube preventing the sense of hearing from attaching to the sound inside it, just as it prevents the sense of sight from seeing what is inside it if there is a visible object, so it does not prove the dependence of the sensation of sound on the carrier air reaching the ear canal. Moreover, we do not concede that the air does not reach the ear canals of those present, and if it were said: if it reached, they would hear, it is prevented because it may depend on another condition. His saying: 'and even if they are equal in distance' is an indication to repel the objection of the author of al-Sahā'if that it is possible that the not hearing is due to the distance of the sound from the limit of hearing at that time, because perception from a distance must have a limit as in vision, so if the perceived object exceeds that limit, it is not perceived. Third: that hearing of sound is delayed from its cause temporally, for we observe the striking of an axe on wood from a distance and we hear its sound, which occurs with it without delay, after a time that varies with proximity and distance, and that is only due to the passage of the carrier air through that distance until it reaches our ear canal. And Imām al-Rāzī objected: that the three proofs reduce to correlation, because their gist is that whenever the carrier air's reaching exists, hearing exists, and whenever it does not exist, hearing does not exist, so it does not give but probability. And it has been mentioned that such proofs require deduction to yield certainty. His saying: 'and we hear its sound which occurs with it without delay' - there is investigation because if the existence of sound is simultaneous with the striking which is an instantaneous impact, it would necessitate that the instantaneous is a complete cause of the temporal or a part of it entailing it, so the previous problem returns. Unless he means by 'with it' more general than what is in its ruling due to the smallness of the intervening time and likewise from the lack of delay. His saying: 'and it is only due to the passage, etc.' - the author of al-Sahā'if objected that it is possible that the not hearing at the time of striking is due to the sound being at that time beyond the limit of hearing. Then when it reaches its limit, it is heard. Yes, if it were established that hearing may be delayed after observing the striking of the axe, whether it is at the limit of hearing or not, it would be repelled, but its establishment is difficult. Then it may be said: why is it not permissible that this is due to the slowness of the attachment of the sense of hearing and the speed of the attachment of the sense of sight due to another cause without the mediation of the passage of air? So reflect.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument: the author first explains the mechanism of sound perception (undulation of air) and then gives three proofs that sound sensation requires the carrier air to reach the ear. It also includes objections and replies. The passage opens by clarifying that sound perception depends on carrier air reaching the ear canal (الصماخ), not through a single air wave but through a chain of undulations. The author then states that we only say this for several reasons, and proceeds to enumerate three proofs. The first proof (the tube experiment) shows that sound is heard only by the person whose ear is connected to the tube, because the tube confines the carrier air. The second proof (wind inclination) shows that sound moves with the wind, indicating that the air carrying it moves. The third proof (temporal delay) shows that sound from a distant source is heard after a delay, accounted for by the time taken for the carrier air to traverse the distance. Following each proof, the author presents objections from a certain 'صاحب الصحائف' (author of al-Sahā'if) and from Imam al-Rāzī, along with replies. For the first proof, an objection is raised that the tube might prevent the sense of hearing from attaching to the sound just as it prevents sight, but the reply is that the tube's confinement of air is the key. For the second proof, an objection is raised that we sometimes hear sound when wind is not reaching us (e.g., muezzin's sound diverted by wind), but the reply is that any distortion in hearing actually indicates that some air reached the ear. For the third proof, al-Rāzī objects that the three proofs only show correlation, not causation, thus yielding only probability. The author then includes further commentary discussing the exact relationship between the sound and its cause (instantaneous vs. temporal) and additional objections about the limit of hearing and alternative explanations like the difference in speed of sensory attachment. The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect), inviting the reader to ponder.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound perception occurs directly without any medium.
Correction: The passage demonstrates through experiments and logical reasoning that carrier air must reach the ear for sound to be perceived.

Checkpoint
In the tube experiment, why does only the person at the tube's end hear the sound?
A) Because the tube amplifies the sound.
B) Because the tube confines the carrier air, preventing it from reaching others.
C) Because the tube blocks the hearing of others.
D) Because the tube selects a specific frequency.

Key Takeaway
Sound perception necessarily depends on carrier air reaching the ear, a principle established by empirical and rational arguments.

?
Al-Rāzī raises the objection that the proofs are merely correlational, not certain. In your own faith, do you ever struggle with doubts when evidence seems merely probable? How do you seek certainty in matters of belief?
?
The author ends with 'so reflect'. Ask yourself: Have I ever accepted a truth based on indirect signs and rational proofs, even when I cannot see the direct cause? How does this lesson strengthen your trust in reasoning about the unseen?
